Preface
Te present thesis was prepared at the Technical University of Denmark within the Department of
Civil Engineering under the supervision of Assoc. Prof. Luisa Giuliani, Asst. Prof. Evangelos Katsanos
and Assoc. Prof. Varvara Zania.
Te work was carried out in the period August - December 2018. Te thesis marks the completion of
the Master of Science in Civil Engineering and represents the equivalent of 30 ECTS points.
Kongens Lyngby, January 01, 2019
Zolt´an Kisfaludi-Bak

==PAGE:4==
Abstract
Current structural design codes do not cover the design of buildings subjected to post-earthquake ﬁres
(PEF), e.g. [1]. Te eﬀects of seismic motion and ﬁre are investigated as two statistically independent,
uncorrelated accidental actions and therefore their eﬀects are not super-imposed. In reality, it has been
proven[2] that the risk of ﬁre increases afer the occurrence of an earthquake due to e.g. electrical and
gas-related failures.
Te combined eﬀect of the ductile behaviour of structures causing second order eﬀects and the damage
of ﬁre insulation during ground motions can lead to the reduction of the structure’s ﬁre resistance. Te
remaining resistance of the structure might not be suﬃcient to resist a ﬁre for the required design time.
Terefore, even if an adequate ﬁre resistance is assured in normal conditions, the ﬁre following earthquake
may cause the structure to collapse.
Te current study focused on assessing the performance of an insulated 2D steel frame when subjected
to ﬁre following earthquake excitations. Plenty of studies focusing on the post-earthquake behaviour of
non-insulated steel frames have proven that material degradation and second order eﬀects have an eﬀect
on the ﬁre resistance of the structure, but with a minor magnitude, [3], [4], [5]. Contrarily, there is very
litle research done on insulated steel frames. Tese studies have all assumed a uniform level of insulation
damage caused by a previous earthquake and investigated the eﬀect on the ﬁre resistance. Risco et al., 2018
[6] performed one of the few studies focusing on insulated steel frames. In this work, the ﬁre resistance
of a steel structure was related to a uniform decrement of the thermal resistance of the ﬁre insulation. In
particular, the study indicates that the ﬁre resistance of beams is not aﬀected if the decrement of insulation
thermal resistance is lower than 25%. Signiﬁcant ﬁre resistance reduction of the column can be observed
at a smaller reduction of insulation thermal resistance.
However, this parametric study is hardly applicable to real cases, where the insulation damage is probably
smaller but localised at the most solicited locations along the elements. Terefore, the present project has
been aimed at investigating the ﬁre resistance of an insulated moment-resistant steel frame where the in-
sulation damage is deﬁned based on the dynamic movement of the structure and applied only in the areas
where excessive deformations occur during ground motions. Structures insulated with spray applied ﬁre
protective coating were the main focus, but the conclusions can be extended to other type of insulation as
well, e.g. intumescent paint.
In the initial step, a ﬁve storey, three bay, moment resistant steel frame was designed according to
current structural design codes, [1], [7] and [8]. Te appropriate level of earthquake hazards were deﬁned
in form of time history records. A sequential numerical analysis scheme was adopted in OpenSees ﬁnite
element code. In the ﬁrst step a simple static analysis was performed according to the design loads. Te
gravity analysis was followed by an earthquake time history analysis, performed for a set of 7 diﬀerent
ground motions. Two earthquakes were selected for the post-earthquake ﬁre analysis. Te damage pat-
tern of ﬁre insulation was deﬁned based on the structure’s local deformations during the selected ground
motions. Te damage of the insulation was assumed to occur at locations where the structural elements
have experienced deformations during the earthquake higher than a limiting value. Te insulation damage
iii
==PAGE:5==
Master Tesis
Fall Semester - 2019
limit strain was obtained from an experimental study reported in literature, [9] and had a value of 3ϵy.
Te earthquake was followed by a ﬁre, which was assumed to ignite afer the ground motion was over.
A standard ﬁre curve was chosen for the modelling of the ﬁre in order to get general and comparable re-
sults, independent on the speciﬁc fuel and compartment properties. As the insulation damage was deﬁned
locally, it was necessary to complete a heat transfer analysis prior to the ﬁre analysis to understand the
distribution and development of temperatures during ﬁre along structural members partly insulated and
partly with insulation damage. Te temperature histories found from the heat transfer model were used to
run a non-linear transient ﬁre analysis. Te location of the ﬁre was considered to be in the middle bay of
the ground ﬂoor. Te so found ﬁre resistance was then compared with the ﬁre resistance of the insulated
frame prior to the earthquake analysis and conclusions were drawn based on the results.
Total nine thermal analysis were performed. Two of these were done on the initial structure without
earthquake, while the other seven were post-earthquake ﬁre analysis. Te PEF analysis were completed
for the two earthquakes selected based on the insulation damage levels: the most demanding earthquake
and an average one.
Te results indicated that insulation damage on the beam reduced signiﬁcantly the time until element fail-
ure, but did not aﬀect the global collapse time. Reduction of minimum 50% was found to happen in case of
the ﬁre resistance of the beam, but the precise reduction was not possible to deﬁne as in the reference (ﬁre
only) case global collapse has happened before the failure of the beam, therefore its initial ﬁre resistance
was not known. In case of the column, a huge inﬂuence of the insulation damage was noticed on both
element failure and global collapse times. Reduction between 50% and 63% was found to occur as a result
of the damage insulation afer the earthquake.
All of the known previous studies, e.g. [6] made for insulated steel frames were based on a uniform
level of ﬁre insulation damage. Te uniform insulation damage levels were applied in these studies by
reducing the thermal resistance of the insulation material uniformly over the entire length of the struc-
tural members. Equivalent uniform damage levels were calculated for the beam and columns based on
the ﬁre resistance reduction found in this study due to local insulation damage. Te equivalent uniform
damage levels had the meaning of the uniform level of insulation damage, which would produce the same
reduction of ﬁre resistance as the local damage identiﬁed in this study. Te results showed that while in
reality only 4% of the surface area of the column insulation and 10% of the beam insulation, the equivalent
insulation damage levels found were 84% and 60%. Tis huge diﬀerence is due to the fact that these studies
disregard the fact that local damages of ﬁre insulation occur exactly in the most solicited locations along
the elements.
It has been concluded that the assumption of a uniform insulation of damage levels in previous studies
is not adequate as damage of insulation is expected to happen in the most critical areas. Delamination
of sprayed protective coating even in small areas can greatly reduce the ﬁre resistance of the structure,
even though the insulation is intact on most of the surface of the element. Insulation damage at the plastic
hinge areas of the beams reduces signiﬁcantly the ﬁre resistance of the beam element, but does not have a
signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the progress or time of collapse. On the other hand, insulation damage at the base
of ground ﬂoor columns have a big eﬀect on the ﬁre resistance and development of global collapse of the
entire structure. Te ﬁre resistance of the structure is reduced by 54% in case of earthquakes producing
suﬃcient deformations at the base of the column.
Furthermore, OpenSees was found to be an eﬃcient tool for running multi-hazard analysis, with some
limitations related to available material models and heat transfer possibilities. OpenSees proved to be a
powerful sofware used for non-linear ﬁnite element modelling, with a wide range of algorithms, analysis
options and capacity of programming analysis tailored to the user needs.

==PAGE:15==
Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation
Currently, structural design codes do not cover the design for buildings subjected to post-earthquake
ﬁres. Te eﬀects of seismic motion and ﬁre are investigated as two statistically independent, uncorrelated
accidental actions and therefore their eﬀects are not super-imposed. In reality, it has been proven that
afer an earthquake the risk of ﬁre increases, due to for example electrical and gas-related failures.
Seismic design codes allow a certain level of ductility of the structure. Te codes prescribe the need
for strong columns and weak beams allowing the formation of plastic hinges only at the beam ends and
potentially at the base of the ground level columns. Such design allows for permanent deformations afer
an earthquake. Permanent deformations can cause second order geometrical eﬀects and by that increase
forces/bending moments acting on the elements. Due to this eﬀect and due to the damage of ﬁre insulation
during ground motions, the ﬁre resistance of the structure might be reduced. In such cases, the remaining
resistance of the structure might not be suﬃcient to sustain a ﬁre or if greatly decreased, it might not resist
enough time for the ﬁre ﬁghters to access the building. Terefore, even if an adequate ﬁre resistance is
assured in normal conditions, the ﬁre following earthquake may cause the structure to collapse before the
design ﬁre resistance time.
Risk of ﬁre afer earthquakes
Te phenomena of post-earthquake ﬁres are deﬁned as low probability with high consequence events
[10]. A demonstration of this aspect was the 1995 Kobe, Japan M6.9 devastating earthquake followed by
approximately 142 ﬁres, developed into several large conﬂagrations. In case of 2011 Fukushima M9 earth-
quake, the ground motion was shortly followed by ﬁres as well as a tsunami and a nuclear accident. A
number of 278 events consisting of inland and coastal ﬁres were reported, resulting in 15,898 deaths, 6155
injured and many missing [2]. From the described examples, it can be seen that post earthquake ﬁres are
accidental events which cannot be disregarded from the structural design in seismic regions.
Non-insulated moment resistant steel frames
Te present study will refer to several research papers made within the topic of post-earthquake ﬁre
design. Della Corte et al. [3] showed that the reduction of ﬁre resistance due to previous earthquake is less
than 10% when unprotected moment resisting steel frames are designed to both ULS and SLS conditions.
Zaharia and Pintea, 2009 [11] showed that unprotected steel buildings designed for stronger seismic
actions have a signiﬁcant additional resistance in case of ﬁre. Tis additional resistance is due to the fact
10
==PAGE:16==
Introduction
Fall Semester - 2019
that the structural members have a low utilisation ratio in case of buildings designed for strong earth-
quakes. As a result of the reduced utilisation ratio, the critical loads for columns and beams are reached
much later during a ﬁre than it would be reached if the building was not designed for earthquakes. How-
ever, frames which sustained permanent damage afer an earthquake have reported decreased resistance.
G. Risco et al., 2018 [5] concluded that the permanent deformations or residual stresses due to earth-
quakes do not have a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the ﬁre response of non-insulated, moment resisting steel
frames. Further, in some cases the stress redistribution afer permanent deformations seemed to have an
unexpected eﬀect, of slightly increasing the time until collapse.
Insulated moment resistant steel frames
Yassin et al., 2008 [12] concluded that the passive ﬁre protection of the building is most likely damaged
by the horizontal movement, while the post-earthquake ﬁre performance of the structure is reduced by
the lateral load induced deformations.
Risco et al.,2018 [6] investigated the eﬀect of loss of insulation due to earthquake on two multi-level
moment resisting steel frames. Te reduction of insulation was modelled as uniform percentage reduction
of thermal resistance of insulation on the elements. Te reduction of ﬁre resistance was proven to be sig-
niﬁcant when signiﬁcant amount of insulation was damaged due to earthquake. Te study shows that loss
of insulation has an eﬀect on the development of the collapse mechanism.
Arablouei et al., 2016 [13] conducted a thermal-structural analysis in which the consequences of ﬁre
insulation delamination was investigated for moment resisting steel frames under seismic loading.
Delamination of sprayed ﬁre insulation occurs in the parts of structure with the highest stresses. In case of
steel frames exposed to ground motions, these appear in the plastic hinge region of the beams. Te study
shows that delamintaion of insulation on beams of 25% in the plastic hinge region can signiﬁcantly reduce
the time to failure in case of a post-earthquake ﬁre.
Bonding of passive ﬁre protection materials
in another study, Braxtan et al., 2011 [9] has investigated the bond performance of sprayed ﬁre pro-
tective materials on steel plates subjected to tensile yielding. Te study has shown that de-bonding and/or
cracking of the spray applied ﬁre protective materials is expected at loading beyond yields. Fibrous wet
mix materials tend to remain as an integral unit and de-bond from the surface of the steel when strains
become large and strain compatibility at the interface of the ﬁre protective material and steel is lost. Dry
mix sprayed materials have the tendency to crack to accommodate large deformations when strains in
the steel become large. Furthermore, it has been concluded that the wet mix ﬁre protective materials have
a higher adhesive strength than dry mix and they also maintain bonding to the steel at higher strain levels.
From the above it can be concluded that important discoveries were made for unprotected steel frames,
but very litle research has been made on ﬁre protected steel frames. Moreover, the studies presented have
assumed a uniform damage of insulation, without relating damage levels to the performance of the struc-
ture during seismic motion. Te need for a detailed study focusing on insulated moment resistant steel
frame was identiﬁed with special focus on the modelling of the insulation damage as a results of ground
motions.
11
==PAGE:17==
Introduction
Fall Semester - 2019
FEM sofware
Most of the previous numerical studies have been done using the sofware called Abaqus. Numerical
models created by the authors of several papers required long time for the analysis to complete and the
need for a new sofware with reduced computational costs was formulated. For this reason an open-source
sofware called OpenSees, known for its modular structure and powerful capabilities, was selected to be
used with the hope of reduction of computational time.
Zhou et al., 2017 [14] showcases OpenSees’ capability for multi-hazard analysis, with analysis of post-
earthquake residual ﬁre resistance of moment-resistant steel frames with cementitious coating.
1.2
Object and Aim
Tis project focused on investigating the performance of insulated steel frames when subjected to ﬁres
following earthquake. Compared to previous studies, a more detailed prediction of damage levels of ﬁre
resistant materials were made. A case study was done on a ﬁve storey, three bay moment resistant 2D
frame protected with spray applied ﬁre insulation. Te eﬀect of the earthquake and damages caused by
the earthquake on the ﬁre resistance and collapse mechanism of the structure was investigated.
Te ultimate goal of the study was to understand more clearly the eﬀects of an earthquake on the ﬁre
resistance of an insulated steel frame and investigate the need for additional design considerations or a
multi-hazard accidental load case in the current design codes including the super-imposed eﬀects of earth-
quake and ﬁre in seismic regions.
Te work is intended to be a case study for performance of OpenSees sofware in a multi-hazard anal-
ysis, with application of assessing the post-earthquake residual ﬁre resistance of insulated steel frames
using thermo-mechanical module.
1.3
Method
Te investigation was made using a systematic approach by performing sequential numerical analysis.
Te sequences are described in the following.
Te ﬁrst step of the analysis was to design the structure according to ultimate and serviceability limit
states given by current design codes and considering individually both, ﬁre and earthquake actions. Te
seismic design of the structure was based on the design response spectrum of the Eurocode, [8].
In the following, a simple static numerical analysis was conducted by considering gravitational loads
acting on the structure including self-weight and imposed loads.
Te following step was a non-linear dynamic seismic analysis, considering non-linear geometry and
non-linear material properties. Te structure was exposed to a set of seven carefully selected time-history
accelerations acting at the base of the structure. Te performance of the structure in terms of permanent
global and maximum local deformations was looked at and damage paterns of the ﬁre protective coating
was identiﬁed for each of the seven earthquakes based on the maximum local deformations experienced
during the ground motion. Two earthquakes have been selected to continue the study with. Te ﬁrst
earthquake was the one generating the largest deformations and greatest insulation damage, while the
second selected earthquake was the one creating mid range damages. Te seismic analysis was prolonged
with a period of four minutes in order to aﬀord suﬃcient time for the structure to stop its motion.
Te next sequence of the analysis was a heat transfer analysis. A ﬁre scenario was selected at the loca-
tion which was thought to be the most critical, at the middle bay ﬁre compartment of the ground ﬂoor. Te
elements from this compartment, beam from the ﬁrst level and supporting columns, were looked at in the
12
==PAGE:18==
Introduction
Fall Semester - 2019
heat transfer analysis. Te insulated elements were introduced in a FEM program with the corresponding
local damages of the insulation. Increasing gas temperatures were applied to the model according to the
ﬁre curve used and the distribution of the temperatures was looked at along the length of the elements to
see the inﬂuence of the unprotected zone over the insulated area. Te temperature distribution histories
along the three elements were then used for the following ﬁre analysis.
Te last step of the sequential analysis was the non-linear transient ﬁre analysis. Te ﬁre action was
given by the temperature curve of the standard ﬁre deﬁned in ISO 834 [15]. Raising temperatures found
from the heat transfer analysis was applied to the elements from the ﬁre compartment and their behaviour
was investigated. Element failure and global collapse times were recorded for each of the performed anal-
ysis. Te results were then compared with the failure times found for the insulated steel unaﬀected by any
previous earthquakes and signiﬁcant conclusions were drawn.
Te initial intention was to perform all sequences of the analysis in the same sofware, OpenSees but
this concept had to be changed during the project due to a limitation of the FEM sofware’s heat transfer
model. Tis part of the analysis was therefore done in Abaqus and the results imported back into OpenSees.
A total of nine full analysis was performed for the studied frame as follows:
• Fire Only (no earthquake) - without ﬁre insulation
• Fire Only (no earthquake) - with ﬁre insulation
• Most Demanding Earthquake + Fire - without ﬁre insulation
• Most Demanding Earthquake + Fire - with ﬁre insulation
• Most Demanding Earthquake + Fire - with ﬁre insulation and ﬁre insulation damage on beam
• Most Demanding Earthquake + Fire - with ﬁre insulation and ﬁre insulation damage on beam and
columns (Case 1)
• Most Demanding Earthquake + Fire - with ﬁre insulation and ﬁre insulation damage on beam and
columns (Case 2)
• Average Earthquake + Fire - with ﬁre insulation
• Average Earthquake + Fire - with ﬁre insulation and ﬁre insulation damage on beam
As the full analysis requires a complex numerical model, the need for validation was formulated. A
series of benchmarks were completed to validate the models built for the main analysis.
Two of the analysis were completed for non-insulated frames: ﬁre only with no insulation and most
demanding earthquake + ﬁre with no insulation. Tis was done in order to see the eﬀect of the earthquake
on the ﬁre resistance of non-protected moment resistant steel frames and to conﬁrm the results found by
several studies done on non-protected steel frames.
13
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2
Teoretical Background
Tis chapter presents the main theoretical considerations on which the investigations were based.
Te ﬁrst section presents some of the key researches performed in the area of post-earthquake ﬁres
and summarises the main ﬁndings of these studies.
2.1
State of the Art
2.1.1
Post Earthquake Fire Records
A review of historical records shows that the damage caused by ﬁres following an earthquake ofen
exceeds the damage of the initial earthquake[16]. A study made by [17] estimated that the post-earthquake
ﬁre was the cause of more than 80% of the total damage in case of the 1906 San Francisco earthquake lead-
ing to more than 3000 deaths. Records show that 140000 casualties happened as a consequence of the 1923
Tokyo earthquake.
Another study made by Faggiano et. al., 2008 [18] estimated that afer the 1923 Tokyo earthquake the con-
tribution of ﬁres to the total damage to be 77%. Later ﬁres following the 1989 Loma Pietra, 1994 Nortridge,
1995 Kobe and 2011 Tohoku earthquakes also caused severe damage, but with less fatalities.
Tanaka et. al., 2012 [2] summarised the records of subsequent ﬁres of major earthquakes with spe-
cial focus on the 2011 Tohoku, Japan earthquake. Almost 16000 fatalities and 4000 missing people were
reported afer the 2011 earthquake with more than 117000 completely destroyed and 178200 severely dam-
aged buildings. Te above consequences of the Great East Japan Earthquake are widely known in contrast
with the consequent ﬁre events. Te study shows that the cause of insuﬃcient awareness and records
towards post earthquake ﬁres is due to that in case of extremely devastating earthquakes and/or tsunamis
where the damage caused by ﬁres, even if serious, may have been overshadowed by the much more ex-
tensive damage of the earthquakes/tsunami waves.
Fire records are not known for neither the 1944 nor the 1946 Japan earthquakes, which caused severe
damage to eastern Japan. Te reason behind the lack of records may be the catastrophic eﬀects of the war
Japan was ﬁghting during these events. Fire records only started to become available lately. Records show
that 278 diﬀerent ﬁres were reported afer the 2011 Tohoku earthquake and indicates that the real number
of ﬁres might have been even higher.
Limited records are available in case of the 1964 Niigata and 1993 Hoakkaido earthquakes. Te records
show that in case of the ﬁrst motion a single oil tank exploded and the spilled oil burned 286 buildings,
while a single house ﬁre lead to destruction of 192 other houses in case of the second one.
Furthermore, the study shows that despite litle data being collected in the past, ﬁres afer earthquakes are
not exceptional but rather ordinary.
14
==PAGE:20==
Teoretical Background - State of Te Art
Fall Semester - 2019
2.1.2
Post Earthquake Fire Studies
Memari et. al., 2014 [19] investigated the behaviour of low, medium and high-rise steel moment resist-
ing frames with reduced beam section connections under post-earthquake ﬁre scenarios. Te investiga-
tions were based on a non-linear dynamic analysis, with elastic-perfectly plastic material and with temper-
ature dependent mechanical, deformational and thermal properties. Eurocode parametric ﬁre curve was
applied on the diﬀerent frames assuming completely damaged state of insulation (as a results of strong
ground motions).
Te study indicated that the global structural performance level of none of the moment resisting frames
was eﬀected by the considered post-earthquake ﬁre scenarios. On the other hand, only symmetric ﬁre
scenarios were considered and the results showed excessive p-delta eﬀects indicating possible collapse for
asymmetric ﬁre. On local level it was found that tension forces developed during cooling phase were in-
signiﬁcant. In case of all three structures some beams had axial force-moment responses higher than their
capacity when considering a ﬁre scenarios acting on one third of the height of each structure.
Yassin et al., 2008 [12] performed an analytic case study for a one and a two storey, one bay moment-
resistant frame, exposed to post-earthquake ﬁre. A model used by Mousavi et al., 2008 [20] was analysed
with temperature dependent material properties and ASTME119 ﬁre curve. Steel beams were exposed to
ﬁre on 3 sides with temperature distribution across cross-section found by SAFIR sofware. Te ﬁndings
of the study state that the two storey frame undergoes an asymmetric failure due to the permanent defor-
mations caused by the earthquake while symmetric failure is expected without lateral deformations, fact
that indicates that at failure the structure undergoes sway. In case of the single storey building, sway can
not occur due to the higher stiﬀness of the structure and the ﬁre induced deformations are much higher
compared to the deformations caused by gravity and lateral loads.
Te study outlines that the permanent lateral deformations have a direct eﬀect on the reduction of the
structure’s ﬁre resistance.
Jelinek et al., 2017 [4] compared the time until collapse due to ﬁre of a structure damaged by an earth-
quake with the one of an undamaged initial structure. Several earthquakes and ﬁre scenarios were consid-
ered in a 2D non-linear numerical analysis, on a 4 storey uninsulated moment-resistant code compatible
[8] steel frame.
Te study outlined that for a low-rise building the second order non-linear geometry eﬀects of the per-
manent deformations do not have a signiﬁcant eﬀect neither on the progression of failure nor the time of
collapse.
Risco et al., 2018 [5] conducted another study on uninsulated moment-resistant steel frames in which
focus was on the reduction of time to collapse and the change in failure mechanism. Investigations per-
formed on two frames (ﬁve and ten storey) showed that the second order eﬀects of non-linear geometries
have equal signiﬁcance in case of the ﬁve and ten storey buildings. Te failure mode of the lower structure
was found to be a symmetric one, while the higher building experienced sway, which might be a serious
concern in case of neighbouring structures. Te results of the study in terms of ﬁre resistance are in ac-
cordance with other investigations [11], [3],[4] as no signiﬁcant reduction of ﬁre resistance was found as
a consequence of permanent deformation or residual stresses afer earthquakes. Furthermore, it has been
found that for some speciﬁc cases, the time until collapse was increased due to the favourable eﬀects of
stress re-distributions.
2.1.3
Spray Applied Passive Fire Protection
In a previously mentioned study, Yassin et al., 2008 [12] concluded that the passive ﬁre protection of
the building is most likely damaged by the horizontal movement, while the post-earthquake ﬁre perfor-
mance of the structure is reduced by the lateral load induced deformations.
15
==PAGE:21==
Teoretical Background - State of Te Art
Fall Semester - 2019
Memari et al., 2018 [21] has proposed a framework for probabilistic performance-base analysis for ﬁres
following earthquakes. Te framework accounts for uncertainties associated with ﬁre hazard, passive ﬁre
protection, gravity load and earthquake intensity.
Arablouei et al., 2014 [22] investigated the SRFM steel interface under seismic and blast loading. Using
a fracture mechanics-based approach the study outlined that inter-facial stresses can open cracks in highly
stressed zones of structural elements. Tese cracks rapidly propagate along the interface between spray
applied ﬁre protection material (SFRM) and steel causing delamination of the insulation layer.
Results from a further study, Kodur et al., 2015 [23] focused on quantifying the eﬀective properties of
spray-applied ﬁre-resistive material to mitigate delamination of ﬁre insulation from steel structures. Te
study concluded that the most important parameters concerning the extent of delamination in the above
cases are the elastic modulus, the thickness and fracture energy of the FSRM material.
Arablouei et al., 2016 [13] conducted a thermal-structural analysis, in which the consequences of ﬁre
insulation delamination was investigated in a numerical analysis for moment resisting steel frames un-
der seismic and blast loading. Te extent of delamination of ﬁre insulation over the structural elements
was based on previous fracture mechanics-based studies, Arabouei et al., 2014. [22]. Results show that
consequence of delamination of ﬁre insulation from beam in the plastic hinge region can be substantial,
concluding that 25% delamination can reduce the failure time from 100 to 64 minutes.
Delamination of sprayed ﬁre insulation occurs in the parts of structure with the highest stresses. In
case of steel frames exposed to ground motions, these appear in the plastic hinge region of the beams. Te
study shows that the delamination of even 25% of the insulation on beams over the plastic hinge region
can signiﬁcantly reduce the time to failure in case of a post-earthquake ﬁre.
Another study, Braxtan et al., 2011 [9] has investigated the bond performance of sprayed ﬁre protec-
tive materials on steel plates subjected to tensile yielding. Te study has shown that de-bonding and/or
cracking of the spray applied ﬁre protective materials is expected at loading beyond yields. Fibrous wet
mix materials tend to remain as an integral unit and de-bond from the surface of the steel when strains
become large and strain compatibility at the interface of the ﬁre protective material and steel is lost. Dry
mix sprayed materials have the tendency to crack to accommodate large deformations when strain in the
steel becomes large. Furthermore, it has been concluded that the wet mix ﬁre protective materials have
a higher adhesive strength than dry mix and they also maintain bonding to the steel at higher strain levels.
Braxtan et al., 2011 [24] described the damage paterns of sprayed ﬁre resisting materials on steel
moment resistant frames exposed to earthquakes. Tis was achieved by conducting real scale experimental
tests to examine the bond of SFRM to steel in the three-dimensional conﬁguration of a moment frame
beam-column connection region.
2.1.4
FEM Sofware
Jingjiang et al., 2012 [25] evaluated the necessary number of section integration points (IP) and ﬁber
integration points in OpenSees considering three diﬀerent type of elements: displacement-based elements,
force-based and plastic hinge elements. Te results were based on responses on both structural and sec-
tional level. Te study showed that for displacement-based elements, 5 section IPs and 6 ﬁber IPs are
suﬃcient to get adequate results. Te ﬁndings of the study were based on a pseudo-static collapse experi-
ment of a 3 storey concrete frame structure.
Morgen et al., 2008 [26] presented the non-linear geometry formulation in OpenSees sofware and
described the sofware design patern in section constitutive models.

==PAGE:22==
Teoretical Background - Earthquake
Fall Semester - 2019
2.2
Earthquake
Design and veriﬁcation of structures exposed to earthquake forces are possible through a dynamic
analysis. One of the simplest forms of dynamic problems are the free vibrations. A structure performs free
vibrations, if the motion of the structure is not disturbed by any dynamic excitation, or in other words no
external forces are applied on the structure. Te free vibration of a structure can be generated by leting
the structure vibrate as a result of introduced initial displacements or velocities.
If a free vibration is initiated, an undamped structure will perform a simple harmonic motion without
having any change in its deﬂected shape. Tis means that all the structure masses would pass through the
equilibrium position periodically and reach extreme displacements at the exact same time. Tis kind of
free motions are described by the normal vibration modes.
Te number of the structure’s vibration modes depends on the number of degrees of freedom of the
structure. Te number of degrees of freedom of a structure is equal to the minimum number of independent
deformations necessary to describe the position of all structural masses in relation to their original position.
Te simplest systems have a single degree-of-freedom (SDOF) and they are characterised by lumped mass,
m supported by a weightless element with stiﬀness k.
In 2D any single node has three degrees of freedom. Two degrees are related to the two perpendicular
directions of the plane, while the third degree is the rotation of the node in the plane. In real structures the
nodes do not just appear individually, but they are connected to each-other, restraining some of the defor-
mation of the nodes and therefore reducing the number of degrees of freedom (DOF). Te total number of
DOFs of a structure is dependent on the boundary conditions and constrains of the structure.
Te natural modes are characteristics of free vibrations, therefore they are related only to the mass and
stiﬀness of the structure, being independent of any other conditions, and therefore they are a characteristic
of the structure. For an undamped linear MDOF system the following equation describes the motion of
free vibration:
[m]¨u + [k]u = 0
(2.1)
Where:
• m is the mass matrix;
• k is the stiﬀness matrix;
• u is the displacements vector;
• ¨u is the accelerations vector.
Te natural period of the vibration mode n, Tn is the duration of one cycle of the simple undamped,
harmonic motion and it can be expressed in relation to the natural circular frequency, ωn as:
Tn = 2π
ωn
(2.2)
Te vibration mode with the smallest natural frequency and largest period is deﬁned as the fundamental
mode of vibration.
In reality, the dynamic problems are much more complex as not only external forces act on the structure
but the structural response is dependent also on damping. Damping is the property of the building due to
which some of the dynamic energy gets absorbed. Figure 2.1 shows an idealised two storey frame subjected
to horizontal dynamic force p(t).
17

2.2.1
Rayleigh Damping
Damping is the capacity of the structure to dissipate energy from dynamic motions. It has a beneﬁcial
eﬀect as it lowers the deformations and forces of the structure. Hence, it is important to understand
its magnitude, on one hand, to be able to produce cost eﬃcient design, but on the other hand, not to
overestimate its capacity and as a result under-design structures.
Classical damping is an accepted idealisation of the damping capacity of simple, uniform material
systems. It can be expressed in two diﬀerent ways, as mass-proportional or stiﬀness-proportional damping,
see Equation 2.19. In both of these formulations, the damping matrix is expressed as to be equal to a given
proportion to the mass or stiﬀness matrices. Stiﬀness-proportional damping can be interpreted as the
dissipation of energy due to storey deformations, while mass-proportional damping can be understood as
the dissipation of energy due to friction of the global structure (not internal, for example due to air).
c = a0 · m
c = a1 · k
(2.19)
If a0 is know, then the damping aﬀerent to each mode of vibration can be expressed in terms of the
modal damping ratio:
ζn = a0
2
1
ωn
(2.20)
If a speciﬁed value of the damping ratio is to be obtained for any of the modes, then the coeﬃcient a0
can be found by:
a0 = 2ζnωn
(2.21)
Similarly, stiﬀness-proportional damping can be writen as:
Cn = a1ω2
nMn
and
ζn = a1

(2.22)

a1 = 2ζn

In reality, neither of the two damping methods accurately model the damping properties of a structure.
Experimental studies show roughly same damping ratios for several vibration modes, while the above
methods provide greater variations of modal damping ratios. For this reason was a more complex model
developed by Rayleigh. Rayleigh damping combines the mass- and stiﬀness-proportional damping into
one.
c = a0m + a1k
(2.24)
Te mass and stiﬀness proportional damping together with the Rayleigh damping are shown in Figure
2.3.

2.3
Fire
Properties of steel materials such as strength and stiﬀness degrade at increased temperature. Terefore,
structural elements made of steel have a lower capacity at increased temperature than the design capacities
obtained at normal, ambient temperatures (usually referred to 20°C). Hence, it is necessary to investigate
the performance of structures in an accidental case, when a ﬁre induces increasing temperatures in steel
members.
Tere is a very low probability to have a ﬁre event exactly in the situation when the building is loaded
to its full capacity, therefore ﬁre design is performed with a diﬀerent set of reduced load factors.
Another eﬀect of increasing temperature in steel structures beside changes in the material properties
are the thermal elongations of the element. At increasing temperatures steel expands, causing elements to
elongate which consequently causes additional forces in case of elements with restraints.
In order to model the structural response at elevated temperature, two aspects have to be know: the
development of the ﬁre, involving the increase of steel members’ temperature and the material degradation
properties. Tese will be discussed in the following sections.
2.3.1
Fire Model
Fires are usually modelled through a time-temperature ﬁre development curve. Several temperature
developments are given by diﬀerent standards, but the main resemblance is that they represent the four
phases of the ﬁre development: incipient, growth, burning and decay.
Figure 2.4: Typical ﬁre development curve
Te ﬁrst, incipient stage consists of the heating of the fuel until the ignition occurs. Tis pre-ignition
phase can last from a few milliseconds to several days. Te next phase is called growth and it starts when
the fuel gets ignited. Fuel ignition can cause combustion or ﬂaming combustion, which generates smoke
and releases energy. Tis release of energy is called heat release rate. In this phase the temperature is
further heated. Growth is fuel controlled and it is deﬁned by an increase of the heat release rate.
Te next phase starts when the ﬂashover happens. Flashover is the transition point between having
a small object oriented ﬁre and a ﬁre overtaking the whole room, by having all combustible materials
ignited. Fires in the post-ﬂashover state are called fully developed ﬁres and are particularly interesting
in civil engineering as the structural stability and integrity can be aﬀected. Signiﬁcant part of the post-
ﬂashover state is called the burning phase, being controlled by the ventilation. Tis means that the ﬁre is
controlled by the amount of air/ventilation available as the fuel pyrolysis.
Te last phase is called the decay phase and it starts when a high amount of the available fuel is burned
(around 80%). Te phase lasts until all fuel is completely burned. Tis phase can last for several days/weeks.
Parametric ﬁre curve is a time-temperature relationship which describes the development of the ﬁre
if parameters such as opening factors, enclosure area, heat release rate and thermal inertia of enclosure
surface are know. As these parameters are building speciﬁc, a more generalized ﬁre model was used.
22
==PAGE:28==
Teoretical Background - Fire
Fall Semester - 2019
A widely used time-temperature development is given by the standard ﬁre curve ISO834. [15]. Te
ISO834 temperature development is represented in Figure 2.5 and is obtained by the equation:
T = 345 log1 0(8 · t + 1)
(2.27)
Figure 2.5: ISO 834 Temperature Curve
Tis curve is not a realistic ﬁre curve as it models only the post-ﬂashover state, by skipping the growth
and decay parts and modelling the most signiﬁcant post-ﬂashover part only, when the temperature in-
creases rapidly, without decay.
2.3.2
Material Degradation
Strength and stiﬀness properties of steel materials are ofen described by their stress-strain curves. At
elevated temperatures, these properties of the steel elements are degraded and therefore an updated stress-
strain curve is used to make structural calculations for elevated temperatures. In general properties are
given at 20°C. Eurocode accounts for material degradation by applying reduction factors on the general
stress-strain curve of the material.
Figure 2.6: Modiﬁed stress strain curve
Stress-strain curves provide some very important parameters of the material, such as the proportional
limit (stress due to which the material is not behaving elastically), yield strength and modulus of elasticity.
See Figure 2.6.
23
==PAGE:29==
Teoretical Background - Fire
Fall Semester - 2019
EN1992-1-2:2004 [27] provides reduction factors for the proportional limit, fsp,θ
fyp , yield strength, fsy,θ
fyk
and initial modulus of elasticity, Es,θ
Es . Tese can be used to modify the material properties when exposed
to elevated temperatures.

Another property of steel materials is their elongation when exposed to high temperatures. Te mate-

the elongation and original length.

2.3.5
Termal Bowing
During real ﬁres there is a very low chance that elements will be exposed to uniform temperatures.
Te temperature of an element during ﬁre depends on many factors such as exposure to ﬁre, construction
method, ﬁre protection, geometry, the material they are made of, but also the surrounding gas tempera-
ture. As it is impossible to have all this factors constant in the whole compartment, thermal gradients are
expected. Concrete in general has very low heat transfer rates and this causes that for example slabs and
beams from the ceiling are ofen exposed to very high temperature gradients as the surface exposed to ﬁre
will be at much higher temperatures than the surfaces from the other sides of the elements. As a result,
the exposed surfaces expand much more than the surfaces from the other sides inducing bending in the
member. Tis eﬀect is called thermal bowing, see Figure 2.10.
Figure 2.10: Simply supported beam exposed to uniform thermal gradient
Termal bowing can appear also at connections between diﬀerent materials, for example in composite
beams due to the large diﬀerence between the temperature and material properties of steel and concrete.
For a simply supported beam, the temperature gradient can be writen as:
Ty = T2 −T1
d
(2.35)
Te curvature induced by the gradient is therefore:
φ = α · Ty
(2.36)
Due to the curvature of the beam, the horizontal distance between the ends of the beam will reduce,
therefore it must be accounted for when calculating the thermal strains. Te magnitude of this reduction
can be expressed by the reduction strain given in Equation 2.37.
ϵφ = 1 −sin(l · φ/2)
l · φ/2
(2.37)
Termal bowing was not accounted for in the current analysis as it was considered that a uniform
temperature acts on the beam and column elements, without any thermal gradient. In the following the

Teoretical Background - Fire
Fall Semester - 2019
combined action of thermal expansion and bowing is presented in order to describe the general failure
mechanisms during ﬁre.
2.3.6
Combined Termal Expansion and Bowing in Laterally Restrained
Beams
In structures, large deﬂections are associated with the loss of strength and usually related to the ambi-
ent conditions. Tis interpretation can be misleading in case of ﬁre. Both thermal expansion and thermal
bowing mechanism generate large deﬂections, but the stress state associated with a member subjected to
the varying combination of these two mechanisms is not unique for a given deﬂection and a large range
of stress states exist. Te actual stress state depends on the material properties, restraint conditions and
temperature distribution in the element.
Te members undergo large deﬂections, because they try to accommodate the additional length gen-
erated by thermal expansion, given that its longitudinal expansion is restrained.
Te eﬀective strain was deﬁned to express the combined eﬀect of thermal expansion and thermal
bowing.
ϵeff = ϵT −ϵφ
(2.38)
A wide variety of responses can occur depending on the value of ϵeff. Positive values mean that the
eﬀect of the mean temperature rise is dominant from the two mechanism and implies compression, while
negative values mean that rather the eﬀect of thermal gradient is the dominant one and it implies tension.
Te value of 0 means a theoretical case when no stresses are in the beam.
Case 1 - ϵeff = 0
In such case all thermal strains get transformed to displacements and the deﬂection will be purely due
to thermal bowing. Te mid-spam deﬂection can be obtained by:

2.3.7
Catenary Action
In general, structural ﬁre design codes are based on capacity veriﬁcation assuming small displace-
ments. During ﬁre, steel elements experience large deﬂections which are not taken into account during
the design. Large deﬂections are accepted as long as they don’t induce structural collapse and they can
have beneﬁcial eﬀects.
One of these eﬀects is the catenary action. When the beam reaches it’s ﬂexural capacity at rising tem-
peratures it requires supplemental resistance which is obtained by the catenary conﬁguration. Catenary
conﬁguration can develop in ductile structures, where large deformations are allowed in which the extra
loads are supported by vertical components of axial forces that develop in the beams.
At high temperatures, this means that the beam will deﬂect signiﬁcantly and resist most of the loads
by its catenary forces rather than ﬂexural stiﬀness. Te so obtained capacity of the beam is higher, as the
resistance is not given by the material strength but rather by the increasing deﬂection.
As the catenary action introduces supplemental axial forces in the beams, extra considerations have to
be made for the adjacent structures as the catenary action imposes additional forces on the surrounding
elements as well.
Te deﬂection of the beam and magnitude of catenray forces is highly dependent on the grade of axial
restraint. Low axial restraints mean small deﬂections and therefore low magnitude of axial forces and
additional bearing capacity. High axial restraints will allow huge deﬂections, which are associated with
high catenary forces. Te disadvantage of this beneﬁcial eﬀect are the imposed forces which have to be
supported by the surrounding elements .
Wang et al. [28] has proposed a methodology to determine the deﬂection and forces of a beam with
catenary action. Te deﬂection and equilibrium diagrams of a steel beam with non-uniform temperature
distribution are shown in Figure 2.11.
28
==PAGE:34==

Fall Semester - 2019
Figure 2.11: Beam deﬂection and equilibrium diagrams

2.3.8
Material Non-linearity
Materials are in elastic state when there is a clear linear correlation between the applied forces and the
deformations. Furthermore, deformations disappear when the forces are released, the body returns to its
original state without any permanent deformations.
In the plastic state, the material’s yield strength is exceeded and permanent deformations appear. Un-
like in the elastic state, deformations do not disappear with the disappearance of the loads. Te same linear
relation between stresses and strains are not valid anymore.
Figure 2.12: Termal elongation
Figure 2.12 presents the strain (top) and stress (botom) distribution in the mid-span cross-section of
a steel beam exposed to uniform load. In Figure 2.12(a) the load has reached the elastic limit. Te entire
cross-section was elastic until this stage. In Figure 2.12(b) the section starts to plasticize as ﬁbers start to
reach the plastic limit, while deformations of the beam continue. In Figure 2.12(c) even more ﬁbers get
plasticized, deformations continue more without any increment of the maximum stress. In Figure 2.12d
the maximum load is reached. Any further increment of the load would cause collapse.
30
==PAGE:36==
Chapter 3
Case Study
Te main study consists of the analysis of a ﬁve storey, moment-resistant steel frame, with focus on
the eﬀects of a previous earthquake on the ﬁre resistance of the structure. Te case study was based on
the analysis of a frame previously investigated by several studies, such as [11], [3] and [4]. All studies
looked at the same ﬁve storey, three bay, moment-resistant frame, with minor modiﬁcations of the struc-
tural members. Tis structure was re-designed to meet the SLS and seismic design requirements of the
more recent versions of the Eurocode.
Te structure in focus is shown in Figure 3.1. Te design of the structure is described in Appendix B.
Figure 3.1: Case study - Studied frame
31
==PAGE:37==
Basis of Study
Fall Semester - 2019
Te modelled structure is a 2D simpliﬁcation of a general steel frame. Te dimensional reduction from
a 3D model to a 2D one is necessary as the ﬁre following the earthquake analysis requires a signiﬁcant
computational time due to the highly non-linear property of the analysis. Previous studies reported, that a
full 2D analysis of the structure in focus, including both earthquake and post-earthquake ﬁre, takes more
than 24 hours. A full 3D analysis would mean signiﬁcantly longer computational time, which is not feasible
within the available time limits of the master thesis. Previous studies, such as [11],[18] and [19], showed
that the simpliﬁcation to a 2D model provides suﬃcient accuracy.
3.1
Material Models
Two materials were used in the entire analysis, one for the structural steel members, and another for
the insulation material. Te properties of these two are presented in the following sections.
3.1.1
Structural Steel Properties
3.1.1.1
Strength - Stiﬀness Relationship at 20 °C
Te initial intention of the project was to use the EC 3 [7] based temperature dependant material model
for structural steel members. Tis model contains a smooth transition between the elastic and plastic be-
haviour, described by a curved stress-strain line. Tis material model was implemented in the OpenSees
thermo-mechanical module and was used in the ﬁrst part of the project. Unfortunately, afer some time it
has been noticed that the model deﬁnition in OpenSees has some errors. Te error is related to the capa-
bility of the material to record plastic strains, meaning that no permanent deformations could be recorded
by the speciﬁc material case even at load levels 10 times higher than the yield values. Tis issue does not
appear in case of any of the other material models. Te issue was communicated to the developers of the
OpenSees thermal module and it is being reviewed.
Consequently, another material model had to be selected. Te options for temperature dependent steel
materials are limited in the newly developed thermo-mechanical module, and therefore a bi-linear model
had to be selected. Te stress-strain relationship of the selected module for the main analysis is shown in
Figure 3.2.

studies, the following partial safety factors were used: γG,j = 1.35, γQ,1 = 1.5 and ψ2,i = 0.3.
3.2.2.1

to the frame. Te parameters of the Rayleigh damping, a0 and a1 were selected in such way to meet the
target damping in the frequency range of the most important modes of vibration. Te parameters used are
given in Table 3.4.

3.3
Finite Element Model
Both the initial static ground motion and the ﬁre analysis were run using OpenSees sofware. Te
model was built in 2D space considering three degrees of freedom at each node. A description of the
most important model setings are detailed here. Refer to Appendix A for more detailed description about
OpenSees and regarding the set up of the analysis.
Te main structural members used were Euler-Bernoulli beams. Te main property of these elements is
that their sections are and remain normal and plane to the beam axis during the deformations and they do
not allow transverse shear deformations. Te elements used in OpenSees are called dispBeamColumn.
Tese are objects that allow spread plasticity along the element and are based on the displacement formu-
lation.
Non-linear geometry was considered in the model by deﬁning appropriate transformation methods
for each of the elements. Corrotational transformation was used to model second order eﬀects.
A sensitivity analysis was conducted as part of this study and presented in Appendix D. Te study
focused on the optimal properties of the displacement based beam-column elements. As a spread plastic-
ity model is required for the analysis, single elements can not provide accurate results. As such, meshing
of the elements is required. Te required number of elements was established based on the investigation
presented in Appendix D. It was found that 20 elements forming each beam and column is necessary to
get suﬃciently accurate results in terms of deformations, stresses and plastic hinge lengths. It is desired to
have the length of the biggest element smaller than the height of the smallest cross section, which is met
for the presented geometry.
Te elements are further divided by the element integration points. Element integration points are
points at speciﬁc locations along the element and represent the cross-sectional properties. Integrating
results over these points give the overall response of the element. Te default seting for displacement
based elements is 3. However, as a result of the sensitivity analysis, this number was increased to 5 to
optimise results and to more accurately record the plastic hinge length within the elements. Te distribu-
tion of the element integration points was done in accordance with the Gauss-Lobato integration method.
37
==PAGE:43==
Basis of Study
Fall Semester - 2019
Another aspect of the elements are the ﬁber integration points placed along the height of the sections.
A section had to be assigned to each of the elements. Te sections were made up of ﬁbers. Fibers are
components of the cross section for which numerical results are computed. Each ﬁber has a position, area
and material properties assigned to it. A number of 11 ﬁber integration points were used to be able to
monitor the plastic stress-strain distribution along the cross-section. A general section of a IPE400 beam
is shown in Figure 3.5.
Figure 3.5: IPE400 section with 11 ﬁber integration points
Te eﬀect of the concrete slabs was modelled through the introduction of restraints. Slabs are con-
sidered to provide a large stability in the horizontal direction of movements. Terefore, the restraints
were deﬁned in such way that all nodes from a given ﬂoor above the ground have the same displacements
(equalDOF command) in the direction of the seismic excitation.
In order to account for the existing dead and live loads during ground motions, these were added as
additional masses on the structure. Te masses were deﬁned as density of the elements and the option for
consistent mass matrix construction was chosen.
As a ﬁrst phase of the study, the external static loads were applied on the structure in a reduced (10)
number of steps, as there was no plastic behaviour expected under these loads. Newton-Raphson solution
algorithm and automatic load control integrator were considered for the static analysis.
Te seismic analysis, on the other hand, was done considering a dynamic Newmark integrator. Te
38
==PAGE:44==
Basis of Study
Fall Semester - 2019
acceleration time histories were read in from external ﬁles for each of the performed analysis. Scaling fac-
tors were applied for each of the earthquakes according to the ones found during the earthquake selection
process presented in 4.3.1. Aferwards, a transient analysis was run.
As convergence is not always obtained in the plastic region, the model was set up to try diﬀerent algo-
rithms in case the Modiﬁed Newton did not converge at certain steps. Tis was possible to do in OpenSees
by writing a simple script that allowed the algorithm types to switches during any step of the analysis.
Te optional algorithms used were Newton with Initial Tangent, Broyden and Newton with Line Search.
Te earthquake analysis was prolonged with a period of 4 minutes. Tis additional time period was
necessary to make sure that movements of the building reduce, and the structure becomes stable at the
end of the seismic analysis, and before starting the next step.
Fire insulation damage was identiﬁed based on the seismic results and a heat transfer analysis was run
in Abaqus to obtain the temperature distributions along the elements from the considered ﬁre compart-
ment.
Te ﬁre analysis was run as a continuation of the earthquake and was based on the results of the
heat transfer analysis. Te elevated temperatures were applied to the aﬀected columns and beams in the
form of temperature history, imported from external ﬁles. A static transient analysis was performed using
automated load control integrator. Similarly to the seismic analysis, a convergence helper method was
used. In case of non-convergence at the end of a speciﬁc step, two other alternative algorithms were used,
namely: Newton with Initial Tangent and Newton with Line Search. In case neither of the algorithms
found suﬃcient results, another step was initiated by drastically reducing the step size. Te reduction of
step size was repeated maximum 4 times, with reduction of the maximum step size each time with a factor
between 102 and 104. Table 3.7 contains a list of some of the main properties of the performed analysis.
Table 3.7: Type of numerical analysis performed
Sofware used

Algorithms Used

==PAGE:45==
Chapter 4
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis
4.1
General Method
Te present study on post-earthquake ﬁre analysis is carried out on a sequential basis. Te main
sequences of the analysis are listed below:
• Design of the structure according to Eurocode. Rules and recommendations for general, seismic
and ﬁre design were considered. Furthermore, spray applied ﬁre insulation on structural steel was
designed in order to meet the 60 minutes criteria given by the Italian annexes of the Eurocode. For
details about the design of the structure and a general presentation refer to Section 3 and Appendix
B.
• Static analysis. Static loads were applied to the structure according to the accidental load combi-
nations as per the Eurocode. Loads were applied relatively fast, in ten increments, as the structure
should stay completely elastic under these reduced accidental combination rules. Afer completion
of the static analysis, the loads were deﬁned to remain active on the structure until the end of the
analysis.
• Seismic analysis. Te seismic analysis of the structure was carried out using a set of ground mo-
tions selected based on the Eurocode [8] and recorded for the same soil conditions and tectonic
environment as the buildings was designed for. Te seismic analysis was carried out with an addi-
tional 4 minutes added at the end of the analysis, to make sure that the structure stops it’s motion
before the next step of analysis.
• Deﬁnition of the state of insulation damage. Te state of damage of the sprayed ﬁre insulation
was determined based on the local deformations of the elements located in the compartment in
which the ﬁre scenario was considered. Te strains of the elements were analysed and where these
were greater than a predeﬁned value limit, the insulation was considered to be damaged.
• Heat transfer analysis. Te ﬁre analysis performed in the next step implies the application of
temperature time history to the structural members. Higher temperatures were applied to the ar-
eas where insulation damage was identiﬁed in the previous step, while lower temperatures were
assigned to the insulated areas. In reality, the transition between these areas does not happen sud-
denly, but rather gradually over a transition zone. Te heat transfer analysis was performed to ﬁnd
the distribution of temperatures through the element, which then in the following step were assigned
to the members.
• Fire analysis. Te ﬁnal step consists of the ﬁre analysis of the structure. A ﬁre compartment was
considered within the building in which raising temperatures were applied and a transient analysis
was performed. As a result of the ﬁre analysis, the time to collapse of the structure was found and
compared with the ﬁre resistance of the initial insulated structure without previous earthquake.
40
==PAGE:46==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Eﬀect of earthquakes of ﬁre resistance
Fall Semester - 2019
Te process of the sequential analysis performed:
Figure 4.1: Sequences of analysis
4.2
Eﬀect of the Earthquakes on the Fire Resistance
It is expected to see a reduction of the ﬁre resistance due to the eﬀects of the earthquake. Tere are
three main aspects that are expected to have an inﬂuence of the PEF resistance of the structure:
• Plastic deformations: during earthquake motions the structure is expected to enter in plastic re-
gion and dissipate energy through plastic deformations. Tese plastic deformations represent per-
manent deformations at the end of the seismic motions, which then generate second order eﬀects.
Second order moments appear due to higher load eccentricities. As a result, the static utilisation of
the structure becomes higher and its resistance to ﬁre reduces.
Most of previous studies have focused on this consequence of the earthquakes and have shown that
plastic deformations will reduce the ﬁre resistance, however, this reduction occurs at a very small
level.
Figure 4.2: Earthquake eﬀects - plastic deformations
• Material degradation: the strength and stiﬀness of the material is expected to decrease as a result
of the cyclic loading during ground motions.
It has been proved that the eﬀect of material degradation is very small and can be disregarded.
41
==PAGE:47==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Eﬀect of earthquakes of ﬁre resistance
Fall Semester - 2019
Figure 4.3: Earthquake eﬀects - material degradation
• Fire insulation damage: steel structures are usually insulated with ﬁreproof materials. As a result
of the motion of the building, these ﬁre protective layers can be damaged, e.g. sprayed insulation
can delaminate due to the diﬀerent stiﬀness properties of the insulation material and the steel, while
board insulation can suﬀer physical damage, cracking, disintegration. Buildings are designed to have
suﬃcient ﬁre resistance by using ﬁre protection materials. If these get damaged, evidently the ﬁre
resistance is expected to decrease.
Only few studies have focused on the PEF of insulated steel frames. Risco et al., 2018 [6] and Suwondo
et at., 2006 [30] are two studies that dealt with the problem of ﬁre insulation damage during PEF
using a simpliﬁed ﬁre insulation damage deﬁnition. Te studies have not considered the deformation
history of the building during earthquakes, but have considered an assumed level of damage of the
insulation. Several levels of insulation damage were considered, e.g. 10%, 25% and 50%, and it has
been investigated to which extent these damage levels have reduced the overall ﬁre resistance of the
structure. No direct connection was considered between the deformation of the structure and the
local damage of the insulation.
Figure 4.4: Earthquake eﬀects - insulation delamination
Te present study focuses on the eﬀects of the plastic deformations and ﬁre insulation damage, as it
has already been proven in the previously mentioned studies that the eﬀect of material degradation can
be disregarded. In contrast with previous studies, the damage criteria of the ﬁre insulation is deﬁned to be
dependent on the actual deformations of the structure due to ground motions. Delamination criteria was
42
==PAGE:48==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Seismic Analysis
Fall Semester - 2019
based on previous laboratory testing results, in which the delamination of sprayed insulation was deﬁned
to be dependent on the envelop of local strains of the elements during motion.
4.3
Seismic Analysis
Te evaluation of structural response during earthquake motions is done by applying several earth-
quakes. Te structure is expected to experience plastic deformation due to the motion of the frame. Tis
occurs through the formation of plastic hinges at the ends of the beams and potentially at the base of the
ground ﬂoor columns. Te main interest of the seismic analysis is to record the permanent deformations
of the structure and also the local deformation of the elements, to which later insulation damage states
can be assigned.
Eurocode 8 [8] states that the design and assessment of buildings can be done using time histories if
the structures response is investigated for a set of speciﬁcally selected ground motions. A number of 7
ground motions are recommended to be used, whose mean response spectra meets the 90% of the elastic
response spectra given by the code in the range of 0.2T and 2T, where T is the fundamental period of the
structure.
Terefore, a set of 7 ground motions were selected based on the criteria deﬁned in the following section.
A transient dynamic analysis was performed for each of the selected earthquakes and the results of the
analysis were presented at the end of this chapter.
4.3.1
Earthquake Selection
Te set of ground motions was selected in such way that all Eurocode requirements are fulﬁlled and
to match as close as possible the response spectra for which the structure was designed for. Te structure
was designed for site class type B, importance factor II and reference PGA of 0.36g. Te characteristics of
the reference elastic response spectra are shown in Table 4.1.
Table 4.1: Reference elastic response spectra
Reference Spectrum
EC, Type 1
Reference PGA [g]
0.36
Viscous damping
1%

II
Site class (EC)
B
Te earthquake records were selected from the Paciﬁc Earthquake Engineering Research Center(PEER)
database, the worlds largest database of ground motion records. In the ﬁrst step of the selection process, a
group of 200 earthquakes were selected that were recorded in similar environments as the one of the de-
signed structure (referring to soil type, tectonic environment). Te selection criteria of this group is shown
in Table 4.2. Ground motions that had epicentral distances closer than 15km to the point of recording were
disregarded in order to avoid near ﬁeld eﬀects. Furthermore, motions with PGA above 0.1 were selected
in order to avoid too high scaling factors at later stages.

4.3.2.1
Most Demanding Earthquake - Irpinia, Italy
Te earthquake producing the highest damage levels was the one from Irpinia, Italy. Te acceleration
time history is given in Figure 4.7. As mentioned before, an additional 4 minutes were added to the seismic
motion before the ﬁre analysis was initiated, but this period is not represented in the following ﬁgures.
Figure 4.7: Irpinia, Italy - Accelerogram
Figure 4.8 describes the motion at the top level of the building. Te graph clearly shows that the build-
ing experiences plastic deformation during the motion. At the beginning of the analysis no displacements
were recorded at the top of the building as the structure was in its initial state. However, at the end of the
earthquake, permanent deformations of around 84 mm were observed.
Figure 4.8: Irpinia, Italy - Displacement at the top of building
A well designed structure should experience plastic deformation during the earthquake in order to
dissipate energy of the earthquake through the formation of plastic hinges. Te formation of plastic hinges
48
==PAGE:54==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Seismic Analysis
Fall Semester - 2019
can be noticed on the bending moment hysteresis shown in Figures 4.9 and 4.10 for the ﬁrst level, middle
beam near the support and at mid-span.
Figure 4.9: Bending moment hysteresis loop at ﬁrst level, middle beam near support
Figure 4.10: Bending moment hysteresis loop at ﬁrst level, middle beam at mid-span
Te formation of plastic hinges can be noticed in the two graphs. A stress redistribution happened
in the structure as a result of the formation of plastic hinges at the beam ends, near the supports. Te
plastic hinges change the stiﬀness matrix of the entire system. As a result, the sections rotate in the area
of plastic hinges and the stress re-distribution occurs. Te bending moments near the support decrease,
while the bending moments at mid-span increase until equilibrium is reached. Te absolute sum of the
negative moments from the supports and positive moments from mid-span give the same total value for
both cases: before and afer the earthquake.
49
==PAGE:55==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Seismic Analysis
Fall Semester - 2019
Te stress re-distribution can be noticed also at the column ends, but this is less signiﬁcant. Bending
moments change in the column as a result of the permanent deformations and the redistribution of the
bending moments in the beam.
Figure 4.11: Bending moment - rotation diagram at ﬁrst level, middle beam near support
Figure 4.12: Bending moment - rotation diagram at ﬁrst level, middle beam at mid-span
Plastic behaviour can be seen also when ploting the bending moment - rotation diagrams. Change
in both bending moment and rotation can be seen at the end of the earthquake in reference to the initial
states.

of the building.
Figure 4.14: Corinth, Greece - Displacement at top of the building
Te same eﬀect of plastiﬁcation can be observed in Figures 4.15, 4.16 and 4.17 as was the case in the
Iripina, Italy earthquake. However, plastic deformations and rotations are smaller than in the previous
case. Te stress re-distribution in the beams decreases the moments at the beam ends and increases them
at mid-span.

4.3.3
Fire Insulation Damage
Previous studies [9], [24] have shown that spray applied ﬁre insulation can delaminate from the surface
of structural steel or crack depending on the material content.
Braxtan et al., 2011 [9] has conducted laboratory bond testing. Te study investigated the bonding of
spray applied dry mix (Portland cement and mineral wool ﬁbre mixture) and wet mix ﬁre insulation (mix-
ture of vermiculite and gypsum) on steel under tension loading. A total of 24 tests were carried out. Te
results show that the spray applied insulation is expected to experience some kind of failure depending
on the material type (wet or dry mix). In general, dry mix insulation had the tendency to detach from the
surface of the steel plate, while cracking was more speciﬁc to wet mix insulation.
From the results, it has been concluded that some kind of damage is expected when local strains exceed
the limiting value 3ϵy. Tis criteria was used for deﬁning insulation damage afer earthquake.
Maximum strains were recorded during each of the 7 earthquakes for all the structural members. Strain
distribution along the cross section was recorded for 100 locations along each element. Insulation was con-
sidered to be damaged at locations where the maximum strain values have exceeded the limit value of 3ϵy.
In such locations, full damage of insulation was considered around the whole cross-section.
Damages were identiﬁed in case of all 7 earthquakes. Te total length of areas with damaged insulation
(strain > 3ϵy) was calculated for each earthquake and summarised in Table 4.8
Table 4.8: Total length of areas with insulation damage

damage of 30cm at their ends. Te columns have only experienced damage at the ground level, with the
maximum damage being recorded on a 18cm long area at the botom of the middle bay columns.

Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Termal Modelling

4.4
Termal Modelling
4.4.1
Aim of the thermal modelling
Te aim of thermal modelling was to identify the expected temperatures along the beam as a result of
local ﬁre protection delaminations.
In the previous chapter the studied frame was exposed to a set of seven earthquakes. Te damages
were recorded in terms of permanent deformations and ﬁre protection material delamination was identi-
ﬁed. As a result of the analysis, it has been found that the spray applied ﬁre protection material is expected
to delaminate in the plastic hinge region of the beams.
As a result, during the post-earthquake ﬁre analysis, it is expected to have no ﬁre insulation in these
areas, while intact ﬁre insulation is assumed in other regions. In terms of modelling, this means a sud-
den temperature change will occur at the interface between the damaged and insulated areas. In reality,
steel has a high conductivity and it is expected to have a transition zone where the temperatures from
the unprotected and protected zones equalise. Te purpose of the thermal modelling was to identify the
temperature development along the beam when exposed to rising ambient gas temperatures and to see
the eﬀect of the unprotected beam ends over the temperature proﬁle of the beam. Furthermore, the eﬀect
of the unprotected beam ends on the temperature distribution in the columns was investigated.
4.4.2
Temperature Distribution
During ﬁre, the temperature of the gases in the ﬁre compartment increase. Te development of the
gas temperature in function of time is given by the standard ﬁre curve in ISO834 and is described by the
following equation and by Figure 4.20
Temperature = 345 · log(8 · Time + 1) + 20
(4.1)
Figure 4.20: Standard ﬁre curve

Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Termal Modelling

transfer, which causes the temperature of the members to increase. Te temperature development for
an unprotected and protected steel member is given by [7] and described by Equation 4.2 and Equation
4.3.

Te above described temperature developments are based on a uniform temperature distribution along

the previous studies. As such, it will be used for the present project as well. In reality small variations
along the cross sections is expected and this kind of temperature distribution was investigated brieﬂy and
is presented in Section 5.3.
4.4.3
Te Eﬀect of Insulation Damage on the Temperature Distribution
Along the Beam
In the previous section, the regions, where the ﬁre protection material is expected to get damaged were
identiﬁed, while the temperature development of unprotected and protected steel members was described
in 4.4.2. Applying the temperature history for the protected and unprotected steel in the deﬁned zones of
the beam would provide a discontinuity in the temperature distribution along the beam at the boundary
between the damaged and undamaged zones as seen in Figure 4.23.
Figure 4.23: Simpliﬁed temperature distribution
57
==PAGE:63==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Termal Modelling
Fall Semester - 2019
In reality, steel has a high conductivity and would not experience such big temperature variation, but
rather would equalise along the beam. Terefore, the need for a heat transfer model was necessary to
compute the real temperature distribution as an eﬀect of the damaged ﬁre protection at the beam ends.
Te initial intention of the project was to perform all sequences of the analysis (seismic, heat trans-
fer and post earthquake-ﬁre) in the same sofware and to be able to have one single complex model that
includes all the information. Unfortunately, during the heat transfer analysis it has been observed that
in the current version of OpenSees it is not possible to perform a complete heat transfer analysis. Te
thermal module of the OpenSees sofware was developed by an individual research group located at Edin-
burgh University, independent from Berkeley - the initial developer of the sofware. Te development of
the thermo-mechanical module started in 2010, while important developments of the heat transfer module
started in 2013 only. Te heat transfer module exists and it is partly functional, but it has been identiﬁed
that the current released version is not capable of performing the necessary heat transfer analysis for this
study.
As a result of this, it has been decided to perform the heat transfer analysis in another sofware than
OpenSees, namely in Abaqus CAE. In order to be conﬁdent in the accuracy and reliability of the heat trans-
fer analysis, several benchmarks were completed. Tese are described in Appendix C.5. Te heat transfer
model was slowly built starting with a simple 2D equivalent section, and ﬁnished with a 3D real section.
Te following sections highlight the ﬁnal results. However, the thermal models leading to these results
are described in the above mentioned Appendix.
Figure 4.24: 2D Heat Transfer - Beam equivalent section - Model
In order to determine the eﬀect of insulation damage on the temperature distribution along the beam,
a 2D model was built considering the same section factor and length as the investigated beam. Convection,
radiation and conduction was deﬁned in the model. Te element was exposed to radiation and convection
from the top and botom. Te middle part of the element was covered by an insulation layer with heat
transfer properties deﬁned in Section 3.1. Te ends of the element, over a region of 30 cm, were not covered
with insulation material in order to simulate the damage of insulation experienced during the earthquake.
Only half of the element was modelled due to its perfect symmetry and to reduce computational cost. Te
geometry of the model is shown in Figure 4.24. Te analysis provided the results shown in Figure 4.25.
Figure 4.25: 2D Heat Transfer - Beam equivalent section - Results

Fall Semester - 2019
It was observed that increased temperatures were recorded in the immediate area of the beam ends,
where insulation was damaged during earthquake. Te purpose of the heat transfer model is to obtain a
temperature distribution along the beam in function of time.
Figure 4.26: 3D Heat Transfer - Beam real section - Coarse model
A 3D model was built as detailed in Appendix C.5 in order to investigate the area close to the damaged
