4.4.4
Te Eﬀect of Insulation Damage on the Temperature Distribution
Along the Column
A heat transfer analysis was required also for the column. Te damage area identiﬁed from the seismic
analysis, see Section 4.3.3, indicated a ﬁre insulation damage of 18 centimetres at the base of these columns
for the most demanding earthquake. Tis length is shorter than the length of a ﬁnite element used in
the main seismic+ﬁre model, which in case of the column was 22.5 cm. Terefore, it was necessary to
investigate the temperature distribution and the length of the transition zone between the insulated and
non-insulated parts of the column, and to obtain an equivalent temperature for the ﬁrst ﬁnite element.
Figure 4.33 shows the model generated for the botom 1 meter of the column afer one hour of ﬁre.
Figure 4.33: Heat transfer model of the column - First 1 meter of the column modelled
A linear temperature distribution was exported for the botom of the column afer one hour of ﬁre
(see Figure 4.34). An equivalent temperature was found by integrating this distribution along the length
of one ﬁnite element of the main model (22.5cm) and dividing it by its length. In such way, an equivalent
temperature history was created for the entire ﬁre duration, maintaining the reduction ratio obtained by
the above calculations. Tis temperature history was used for the analysis where the columns experienced
some level of insulation damage and it was referred to as Case 1. A slight increment of temperature can
be noticed in the area of the second ﬁnite element, but this increment is so small, that it was disregarded
in the analysis.
62
==PAGE:68==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Termal Modelling
Fall Semester - 2019
Figure 4.34: Temperature distribution along the ﬁrst 1 meter of the column at middle of the web
Te above described equivalent temperature on the damaged part of the column is non-conservative.
At the base of the column temperatures reach the non-insulated temperatures, which are higher than the
equivalent one. If the failure of the element occurs in this area, the use of an equivalent temperature is
not conservative. Terefore, a more conservative approach was also considered as an alternative to the
equivalent temperature distribution. Te more conservative approach was based on a full loss of insulation
on the ﬁrst ﬁnite element, assuming that the full length of 22.5 cm was damaged. Te analysis considering
this assumption was called Case 2. Tis is further explained in Section 4. Figure 4.35 shows a simpliﬁed
sketch of the two cases.

4.4.5
Possibilities for Further Studies - Reﬁned Temperature Distribu-
tion Across the Cross-Section
Te current study assumes a uniform temperature distribution across the cross-section. Te real tem-
perature distribution varies across the cross-section as seen in Figure 4.27, 4.28 and Figure C.47. An area
of focus in future studies could be the application of the non-uniform increasing temperatures on the FEM
model according to the heat transfer analysis results. Most FEM programs, such as OpenSees, is capable of
assigning varying temperatures across cross sections. In OpenSees cross sections can be assigned temper-
atures in maximum 9 points. Such detailed modelling of the thermal behaviour could provide interesting
information about the formation of local failure mechanisms and could take into account phenomena like
thermal bowing.
4.4.6
Possibilities for Further Studies - Joint Investigations
Another aspect which could be further investigated is the inﬂuence of the damaged insulation from
the beam ends on the temperature development of the columns they are supported by. Tis mater is de-
pendent on the joint type and practical realisation, as well as the exact shape of the insulation damage.
A simpliﬁed analysis was conducted as part of this study and the results can be seen in Figure 4.36.
Figure 4.36: Simpliﬁed heat transfer model of beam-column joint
Te model was built to get an indication of the magnitude of inﬂuence of the damaged ﬁre protection
on the temperatures of the column. Several simpliﬁcations were considered. One of them was that direct
contact was considered between the column and the beams, while in reality such moment resisting con-
nections are made with the use of end plates and stiﬀeners.
64
==PAGE:70==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Termal Modelling
Fall Semester - 2019
Due to modelling issues, another simpliﬁcation implied that no convection and radiation was applied
on the immediate surface area at the direct contact of the beam-column connection. With the current
simpliﬁed model it was not possible to deﬁne a surface contact between the column and the beam and in
the same time to apply radiation and convection on the this surface. Tis issue can be solved with a more
detailed beam-column joint heat transfer model.
With the above assumptions, the analysis were run and the results are shown in Figure 4.37.
Figure 4.37: Simpliﬁed heat transfer model of beam-column joint - Results
Te result from the above model were compared with the results of a fully protected column without
beam joint, and it has been concluded that the inﬂuence of the loss of protection from the beam ends has
a minor eﬀect on the temperature development of the columns. Tis was expected due to the very small
contact surface between the two elements. Terefore, in the rest of the study, it was assumed that the
damage of the ﬁre protection on the beams has no eﬀect on the temperature development of the columns.
As part of further studies, the heat transfer of the beam-column joints could be investigated. A more
detailed model, with the realistic representation of the connecting elements, including end plates and
stiﬀeners, would provide a beter understanding of the joints during ground motions and heat transfer.
As plastic hinges develop at the beam ends and signiﬁcant deformations appear in the joints during the
earthquakes, some level of ﬁre protection damage can be expected also in this area. Te behaviour of
beam-column connection was not part of this study and a more detailed model is necessary to beter un-
derstand the response in this area during ground motions.
65
==PAGE:71==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Fire Response
Fall Semester - 2019
4.5
Fire Results
4.5.1
Aim of the Fire Analysis
Te aim of the ﬁre analysis is to ﬁnd the answers to the main questions of this study. A series of anal-
ysis were performed in order to see the eﬀect of an earthquake on the ﬁre resistance of the structure.
Te most demanding and average earthquakes were selected based on the permanent deformations
and damage levels of ﬁre insulation, as presented in Section 4.3.3. Afer the earthquake motion, a waiting
time of 4 minutes was assigned to the structure to make sure that the dynamic motion stops. Tis addi-
tional time between the earthquake and ﬁre analysis is appropriate as the standard ﬁre curve used does
not include the early development phase of the ﬁre, and rather represent a post-ﬂashover development.
All models were exposed to the same ﬁre described by the standard ﬁre curve presented earlier. Te
same ﬁre was applied to the non-insulated and insulated structural members of the considered ﬁre com-
partment. Tis was done by inducing temperatures that were calculated based on the ﬁre curve, properties
of the steel members (material, section factor, etc.) and properties of ﬁre insulation.
A diﬀerent approach was used for the insulated cases where part of the insulation was damaged as
a results of the earthquake, as the distribution of the temperatures along the members was not trivial in
these cases. Time dependent temperature distributions over the length of elements were applied to the
FEM model. Te temperature distributions were found from the heat transfer analysis presented in the
previous section.
Table 4.9 contains a summary of the performed analysis.
Table 4.9: List of analysis performed
66
==PAGE:72==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Fire Response
Fall Semester - 2019
4.5.2
Fire Scenario
Earlier studies have focused on non-insulated steel frames in which the eﬀects of diﬀerent ﬁre scenar-
ios were investigated, e.g. [3], [6], [4]. Te results showed that similar eﬀects in terms of reduction of ﬁre
resistance can be concluded for the diﬀerent ﬁre scenarios. Terefore the focus of the present project was
put on the most relevant ﬁre scenario.
Fire was assumed to happen in a single ﬁre compartment of the frame. Te location of the ﬁre was
chosen to be in the compartment where the greatest damage of the ﬁre insulation is expected. Te dam-
age level of the beam insulation is dependent on the applied earthquake: in some cases the beams from
the lowest levels experience the highest deformations, while in other cases the beams from the top ﬂoor
deform the most. In the same time, the variation of damage levels between the diﬀerent compartments
are small for the same earthquake, while the earthquake itself has a much higher eﬀect on the general
damage levels of all beams. Te columns, on the other hand, are in most part non-aﬀected as the seismic
design is based on the consideration of no plastic hinges in the columns, with the exception of the base of
the ground level columns. Terefore, the only ﬁre scenarios in which the columns might be damaged are
at ground level. For most of the considered 7 earthquakes, the highest damage on beams was recorded at
ground ﬂoor, middle compartment. Refer to Section 4.3.3 for more details.
As a result, the ﬁre scenario considered is located in the middle compartment at ground ﬂoor as shown
in Figure 4.38.
Figure 4.38: Fire scenario considered
67
==PAGE:73==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Fire Response
Fall Semester - 2019
4.5.3
Fire Analysis - Fire Only - Non-insulated Frame
Te ﬁre only case was run in order to conﬁrm the results found by other studies for non-insulated steel
frames, to know the ﬁre resistance of the initial structure and to understand the collapse mechanism of
the simple, non-insulated structure.
4.5.3.1
Global Collapse
In the following, the sequence of events leading to global collapse and failure of the FEM analysis are
described. Te FEM analysis has failed in the moment the structural stability was lost and exponentially
accelerating displacements (run-away) were recorder at the middle of one or both of the columns. In reality,
global failure would occur earlier than this stage. In some cases the strains of the beam and/or columns at
the location of plastic hinges were over 100% at the failure of the FEM analysis. Te material model used
has a perfectly plastic range, which meant that the material could experience inﬁnite strains at the same
stress levels, without having any limitations. In general, it is considered that an element can be considered
failed/broken when somewhere along its length it reaches strains of 20%. Tis limitation was not deﬁned
in the FEM model, therefore the analysis continued to run even afer this limit was exceeded, until global
instability happened. Te time of the analysis failure was considered as stability failure and global collapse.
Beams and columns experienced continuous deformation until this point, which is a theoretical behaviour
as in reality they would have failed/broken much earlier.
Failure of the beam was considered to occur in the moment the ultimate strain was reached at the
location of one of the plastic hinges, close to the supports or mid-span. Failure of a column was considered
when the column lost its stability and started to buckle. Te deformations of the structure for each sequence
step are shown in Figure 4.39a-4.40d. All deformations in the mentioned ﬁgures are shown up-scaled by a
factor of 5.
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
Figure 4.39: Fire Only - Non-insulated frame - Sequence of events 1

Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Fire Response

4.5.3.2
Beam Response
Figures 4.41 and 4.42 show the axial force and bending moment development in the beam during the
ﬁre at two diﬀerent locations: near support and at middle of the span. Tese ﬁgures are referenced in the
following sections containing an analysis of the beam response.
Figure 4.41: Axial force and bending moment diagrams for beam near support
Figure 4.42: Axial force and bending moment diagrams for beam at mid-span
Te dashed lines shows the section forces during the ﬁre, while the straight lines represent the sec-
tional axial/bending capacity.
70
==PAGE:76==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Fire Response
Fall Semester - 2019
At the beginning of the ﬁre, when the temperature starts to rise, the beam ends are already plasticised
due to the earthquake. An eﬀect of this is the reduced bending moment of the beam at the support and the
increased moment at mid-span.
It can be noticed that increasing compression forces appear in the beam right afer the ﬁre starts. Tis
is due to the expansion of the member, which is partially hindered by the columns. Te compression forces
increase continuously in the ﬁrst 8 minutes afer which it suddenly starts to decrease. Tis is the point
when the beam reaches 400 degree and the strength degradation of the material starts. Te stiﬀness of
the material starts to degrade at a lower temperature, which contributes to the increment of the bending
moment close to the support. In parallel with this, the bending moment at mid span starts to decrease.
Te reason for this behaviour is that the steel looses its stiﬀness and starts to deﬂect at a higher rate.
Afer 13:47 minutes three plastic hinges form in the beam. Te beam continues to deform in the next 18
minutes, time afer which it reaches the ultimate strain of 20% at mid span. At this strain level it is con-
sidered that the beam would brake/fail. Due to the previously presented properties of FEM modelling, the
analysis continued until global stability of the structure was reached.
As the beam continues to deﬂect, the compression forces decrease up to the point when it reaches 0
and turns into tension forces afer around 19 minutes. Tis eﬀect is called catenary action and consists
of supporting the existing loads by axial catenary forces, rather than bending. For this action to happen,
suﬃciently big deformations are necessary. As the beam starts to experience tension forces, it pulls the
two supporting columns towards each-other.
4.5.3.3
Column Response
Te response of one of the columns was discussed below. Due to the symmetry of the frame, only a
single column is discussed. From the analysis results it was concluded that the two columns in the ﬁre
compartment have a very similar response, with a small shif (magnitude of seconds) between them due
to the diﬀerences of permanent deformations from the earthquake.
Section forces at the base of the lef column were shown in Figure 4.43. In the ﬁrst 8 minutes, the
same behaviour can be observed as in the case of the beam. Te columns starts to elongate, but as the
beams are clamped to them they are restricted to do so, therefore, additional compression forces develop
in the column. Te bending moment development shows a clear increment until 8 minutes that is due
to the expansion of the beam. It was seen observed in case of the beam that the compression forces due
to the hindered thermal expansion increase until this time. Tese forces push the column outward and
cause bending moments. Te same can be concluded from the horizontal displacement graph given for the
middle of the lef column shown in Figure 4.44. At the start of the ﬁre, the horizontal displacements are at
1.64 cm due to the permanent deﬂections caused by the earthquake. Te earthquake was applied from the
lef side of the frame, and caused permanent deﬂections to the right. Tis meant that at the initiation of the
ﬁre, the lef column had a small deﬂection towards the right. As the beam started to expand and push the
joint outward, the columns started to move to the lef, causing the reduction of horizontal displacements
seen in the ﬁrst 8 minutes. Afer this point, the material starts to degrade, the beam compression forces
decrease, the beam continues to deﬂect in the middle and, therefore, the nodes move back to their normal
position. Tis causes the decrease of horizontal displacements and bending moment at the base.
Te joint continues to move back towards its original position and the bending moments decrease to 0.
When the catenary action appears in the beam, the tension forces pull the to columns towards each other
causing small bending moments of opposite sign at their bases.
71
==PAGE:77==
Post Earthquake Fire Analysis - Fire Response
Fall Semester - 2019
Figure 4.43: Axial force and bending moment diagrams for column base
At around 20 minutes, both columns start to buckle and as a result the axial forces start to decrease.
Te buckling can be well seen on the displacement graph as an accelerated displacement of the middle
of the columns happens starting this time. As the buckling continues, ultimate strains are reached in the
column. Te column buckles until a point when the increase of displacements becomes inﬁnite and the
column deforms extensively. Tis moment is well observed at the end of the displacement graph, where
the displacements start to increase abruptly, in a straight line (run-away). Te analysis fails when global
instability happens due to the loss of two columns.
Figure 4.44: Horizontal displacements of column at mid-span

4.5.4
Fire Analysis - Fire Only - Insulated Frame
Similar analysis was completed for the same structure with ﬁre insulation. Te ﬁre insulation was
designed in such way to provide 60 minutes of ﬁre resistance. Te design of ﬁre insulation was based on
Eurocode provisions, which assumes a smooth transition between elastic and plastic regions of the mate-
rial model. On the other hand, the results presented for the FEM models are based on a bi-linear material
model, which slightly overestimates the strength and stiﬀness in the transition zone of the EC model. For
this reason, it was expected to have a ﬁre resistance a bit higher than 60 minutes for the insulated case.
Te sequence of events leading to global collapse is somehow similar as in the non-insulated case, with
signiﬁcant diﬀerence in the failure time of the individual members. Te time to failure of the columns in
the non-insulated case is much longer than the ﬁre resistance of the beam. Tis is due to the fact that the
column has signiﬁcant over-strength due to the design considerations. Te earthquake design imposes
limitations on the maximum storey deformations, and the columns were selected such that they meet the
criteria, but this meant, that the proﬁles selected were bigger than what would be necessary from simple
static/stability point of view. Tese larger proﬁles are the explanation for the additional resistance capacity
of the columns.
Tere was a signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the ﬁre resistance of the non-protected beam and column.
Tis diﬀerence was diminished as ﬁre protective coating was applied for both elements. Fire insulation
was designed for 60 minutes for both elements individually, therefore the failure of the two was expected
to happen around the same time during the analysis.
On the other hand, the ﬁre resistance of the beams and columns were based on simple general element
calculations and did not consider eﬀects such as hindered thermal expansion or additional forces and de-
formations caused by the earthquake on the rest of the structure. Hindered thermal expansion had the
eﬀect of reducing the failure time of the beam (in comparison with the design 60 minutes). In the same
time, the expansion of the beam caused bending in the column, eﬀect which created eccentricities and am-
pliﬁed loads. Tis eﬀect is neither considered in general element insulation design, but it does inﬂuence
negatively the ﬁre resistance of the element.
Te results for both non-insulated and insulated cases are summarised in Table 4.10.

Tere are two main diﬀerences in the response of the insulated frame compared to the non-insulated
one. One of the diﬀerence is that there is no signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the failure time of the beam
and column as a results of the applied insulation, whereas before there was a signiﬁcant diﬀerence. Tree
plastic hinges form in the beam afer 55:38 minutes, while the ﬁrst column starts to buckle at 56:44. Te
diﬀerence between these two was much higher in the non-insulated case due to the over-strength of the
column; the beam failed afer 13:49 minutes, while the column started to buckle only afer 20:21 minutes.
Another diﬀerence is that the global collapse happens earlier than the local failure of the beam as a result
of the excessive deformations of the buckled columns.
4.5.5
Fire Analysis - Most Demanding Earthquake + Fire - Non-insulated
and Insulated Cases
Te ﬁre analysis was repeated for the same structure exposed to a previous earthquake. Te earthquake
- Irpinia, Italy providing the highest damage level was selected and the analysis was run to conﬁrm the
eﬀects of the earthquake on the ﬁre resistance of the non-insulated structure described by previous studies
[3], [11], [4]. Tere are three factors of a pre-ﬁre earthquake which can inﬂuence the ﬁre resistance of a
structure:
• Material degradation - It was presented earlier and found in several studies that the eﬀect of the ma-
terial degradation during earthquakes is insigniﬁcant in regards to the ﬁre resistance of the structure.
• Second order eﬀects - Structures undergone earthquakes can experience plastic deformations and
therefore permanent deformation. Tese deformations induce second order eﬀects (for example
eccentricities in columns) which can increase the loads and theoretically reduce the ﬁre resistance.
Studies made by [3] and [11] showed that second order eﬀects do have an inﬂuence on the ﬁre
resistance, but this inﬂuence is very small. Furthermore, in several studies an interesting eﬀect of
increase of the ﬁre resistance was noticed for several members as a result of redistribution of forces
afer the earthquakes.
• Insulation damage - Eﬀect due to the physical damage, cracking, delamination or physical disinte-
gration of the ﬁre insulation materials.
With the exception of material degradation, all other eﬀects were looked at in this project.
Te ﬁre analysis on the non-insulated and insulated structure exposed to the most demanding earth-
quake were done to conﬁrm the minor magnitude of the second order eﬀects.
Te behaviour during the post-earthquake ﬁre follows closely the sequence of events described for
the Fire Only non-insulated and insulated cases. Table 4.11 tabulates the result for the non-insulated and
insulated post-earthquake ﬁre analysis.

4.5.5.1
Comparison of Non-Insulated Fire Only and Post-Earthquake Fire Cases
A slight reduction in the ﬁre resistance can be noticed when comparing the results of the Fire Only
(see Table 4.10) and the Most demanding earthquake + Fire (see Table 4.11) cases.
Both beam and columns experience a slight reduction of resistance due to the post-earthquake ﬁre.
Te post-earthquake ﬁre results show that three plastic hinges form in the beam 2 seconds - 0.22% earlier
than when no earthquake has happened before the analysis. Similarly, the time until the beam reached the
ultimate strain of 20% was reduced with 19 seconds - 1.24%.
Te failure time of the two columns was similarly reduced by 19-27 seconds, equivalent to 1.52% and
2.21%. In the same time, the time until global collapse is not reduced, but rather elongated with 42 seconds,
2.36%. Tis can be due to the redistribution of forces happening in the frame structure during and afer
the earthquake and due to the beam-column joint rotation. In the same time, it is worth mentioning that
this global collapse time is a theoretical one as the FEM model allows inﬁnite deformations of the element
and collapse happens due to loss of stability under excessive deformations.
4.5.5.2
Comparison of Insulated Fire Only and Post-Earthquake Fire Cases
Plastic hinges form 7 seconds earlier in the post-earthquake situation for the beam, equivalent to a
0.22% time reduction. Te reduction of the columns failure time is between 46 and 51 seconds, equivalent
to 1.36-1.50%. It should be noted that global collapse happens before the local failure of the beams as a
result of the excessive deformations of the buckled columns.
Furthermore, a similar eﬀect of elongation of the theoretical global collapse time can be noticed. In
this case the time of global collapse was prolonged with 22 seconds, equivalent to 0.46%.
As a conclusion, some reduction of ﬁre resistance can be noticed as a results of an earthquake motion
happening to the structure prior to the ﬁre, but with no inﬂuence on the collapse mechanism. Te indi-
vidual failure time of the elements is expected to be reduced by 1-2%. Tese ﬁndings are in line with the
above mentioned studies.
Under these considerations, the magnitude of the inﬂuence is so minor that it can be considered that the
second order eﬀects caused by pre-ﬁre earthquakes are not signiﬁcant, similarly as the eﬀect of material
degradation. Consequently, the only factor which can have major eﬀects on the ﬁre resistance afer an
earthquake is the damage of the ﬁre insulation. Tis was investigated in the following.
4.5.6
Fire Analysis - Most Demanding Earthquake + Fire - Insulated
with Damaged Insulation at Beam Ends
In the following analysis, the damage of the insulation (cracking, delamination) occurring as a result
of dynamic motions during the earthquake was considered. Te temperatures of the members from the
ﬁre compartment were generated based on the damage levels determined afer the earthquake analysis
and the heat transfer model presented in Section 4.3.3 and Section 4.4.

earthquake analysis showed that strains exceed the given criteria of 3ϵy. Te reason behind this is that in
general, the earthquake design is made in such way to have strong columns and week beams, with plastic
hinges at the beam ends and no plastic hinges in the columns, except the base supports. In case of 6 of
the 7 earthquakes studied, there was no plastic hinge formation at the base of the columns, and only in
the case of the most demanding earthquake some delamination has occurred. Terefore, in the ﬁrst case

4.5.7
Fire Analysis - Most Demanding Earthquake + Fire - Insulated
with Damaged Insulation at Beam Ends and Column Base
In the following case, the damage of the insulation at the base of the columns was also considered. Te
seismic design is driven by the approach of allowing plastic hinges to form at the beam ends and the col-
umn base at ground level only. Tis means, that the ﬁre resistance of the structure will only be aﬀected by
a pre-ﬁre earthquake if the ﬁre is initiated at the ground level. In our case the ﬁre scenario was identiﬁed
to be most critical at the ground level, therefore it is expected to see a greater reduction of the ﬁre resistance.
For the most demanding, Irpinia, Italy earthquake, the seismic analysis indicated insulation damage of
18 cm at the base of the column. Tis length is shorter than the length of a FE element in the seismic and
earthquake analysis. Te damage of 18 cm is obtained as the damage criteria is based on local strains and
5 section integration points are placed along the length of the element where stress-strain relationships
can be recorded. Terefore, the length of the damage area can be found to be shorter than the length of an
element. On the other hand, temperature variations can be deﬁned only for whole elements and not for
their integration points. Terefore, it was required to assume a temperature proﬁle for the whole element
knowing that only part of it was damaged. Tis was done in two ways as presented in Section 4.4 and as
described below:
• Weighted average temperature - Considering non-insulated temperature in the damaged area and
an average temperature for the transition zone - conservative approach
• Non-insulated temperature - Simply considering no insulation over the whole element - might be
more relevant if global collapse is caused by element failure at section level in this area
Te two case are further described in Section 4.4.
4.5.7.1
Case 1
Te sequence of events leading to global collapse in the ﬁrst case were the following:
• Te beam and two columns from the ﬁre compartment start to expand as temperature starts to rise
• 13:32 min, Tg = 723°C - First plastic hinge forms at the end of the beam, close to lef support
• 13:45 min, Tg = 726°C - Te second plastic hinge forms close to the right support
• 28:09 min, Tg = 832°C - Plastic hinge forms at the base of the lef column
• 28:13 min, Tg = 833°C - Plastic hinge forms at the base of the right column
• 30:34 min, Tg = 845°C - Ultimate strain is reached at the base of the right column and shortly afer
the same happens in the lef column
• 45:58 min, Tg = 906°C - Global collapse happens as a result of stability failure of the structure
In contrast with all the earlier analysis, the global mechanism has changed when the insulation dam-
age of the column was considered. In this case, the behaviour of the beam is similar to all previous cases,
but the failure mode of the column and global collapse has changed. In the prior analysis, the column
has always failed due to buckling and ultimately due to excessive deformations at its middle height. In
the current analysis, the base of the two columns were exposed to much higher temperatures than the
rest of the members and therefore plastic hinges have formed here. Temperatures continued to raise, the
materials strength and stiﬀness continued degrading until the point when it failed in compression.

4.5.7.2
Case 2
Te diﬀerence in relation to the previous case is that for Case 2 higher temperature values were as-
signed to the ﬁrst element of the column. As presented above, the temperature development considered for
Case 2 was the non-insulated temperature development assuming damage on the whole ﬁrst FE element,
rather than only part of it. Tis approach seams reasonable, as in Case 1 it has been concluded that the
column failed due to local failure at the base due to compression. In Case 1 the temperature assigned to
the ﬁrst element of the column was an equivalent temperature calculated as weighted average, which was
lower than the pure temperature which would develop in a completely non-insulated element. Based on
the seismic results, some of the ﬁrst elements would be completely exposed to ﬁre due to the damage of
the insulation, while some of it would remain insulated. However, if the failure happens locally, then the
representative temperature to be considered is the non-insulated temperature as the beam will fail in the
damaged area.
Table 4.12 summarises the results for all ﬁre analysis performed with damaged insulation for Irpinia,
Italy earthquake.
It can be seen that the response is very similar to the above Case 1, with the only diﬀerence being a
shorter time of failure of the columns. A plastic hinge was formed in the ﬁrst column more than 35 minutes
earlier than when the column buckled in the initial case without prior earthquake. Te column reached
ultimate strain more than 41 minutes earlier than in the initial state (reduction of 62.99%).
4.5.8
Fire Analysis - Average Earthquake + Fire - Insulated
Another set of analysis was performed for an earthquake selected from the set of 7 earthquakes that
caused a medium level of damage. For this purpose the earthquake from Corinth, Greece was considered.
Te insulated case was considered to see the inﬂuence of the second order eﬀects caused by another
earthquake on the ﬁre resistance of the structure. Table 4.13 presents the results for this case. Te values
indicate that the magnitude of the reduction of ﬁre resistance of the individual elements and global struc-
ture is lower than 2%. Furthermore, in case of the beam a slight delay in the formation of a plastic hinge
was noticed, resulting in a ﬁre resistance greater with 1:15 minutes. Tis increase in the ﬁre resistance is
assumed to be due to the re-distribution of stresses as a result of the ground motions and is in accordance
with results found by [5] and [4].

4.5.10.1
Beam Failure
Figure 4.47 shows the failure times of the beam for each of the completed analysis. Te cases with
’***’ symbol were the ones in which global collapse due to failure of the columns has happened before the
ultimate strain of the beam was reached. In these cases the global failure time was indicated as failure time
of the beam.
Figure 4.47: Time until beam failure
Te results indicate that the damage of insulation on the beam reduces signiﬁcantly the time until
failure. It is not possible to quantify precisely the reduction of the ﬁre resistance of the beam, because
in the Fire Only case the global collapse happened before the column has failed. If global collapse time
is considered as failure time of the beam, then a reduction of about 50% can be observed due to the local
damage of the insulation. Furthermore, the magnitude of ﬁre resistance reduction is equally signiﬁcant for
the average and for the most demanding earthquakes, with a relatively small diﬀerence due to the stronger
earthquake.
Even though the ﬁre resistance of the beam was signiﬁcantly reduced in the damaged insulation cases,
the global collapse time was not signiﬁcantly aﬀected by the early failure of the beam.

4.5.10.3
Global Collapse
Figure 4.49 shows the time of theoretical global collapse for each of the completed analysis. It was
named theoretical collapse, as in reality the structure would fail before this time. Te FEM analysis does
not consider failure of the individual elements due to deformations. Te material model is built on a
bi-linear elastic - perfectly plastic model, therefore it allows inﬁnite increments of strains in the plastic
region. Individual elements are expected to brake afer reaching 20% strains. Te FEM program continues
the analysis, even afer this limit is reached, until the point when deformations are so high that the global
stability of the building can not be sustained. Tis time was considered as the theoretical global collapse
time.
Figure 4.49: Time until theoretical global collapse
Furthermore the same tendency can be noticed for the average and for the more demanding earthquake.

4.5.11
Comparison with Studies on Insulated Steel Frames
As mentioned before, all of the know previous studies on insulated steel frames,e.g. [6], were based on
a uniform level of ﬁre insulation damage. In these studies, the level of insulation damage was not related
to the frames performance during ground motions, in contrast, was considered uniform along the whole
element and it was rather an imposed assumption of damage level. Te uniform insulation damage levels
were applied in these studies by uniformly reducing the thermal resistance of the insulation material over
the entire length of the structural members.
Te insulation damages considered in this study were local damages, based on the deformations recorded
during the ground motions. Te areas where the deformation were suﬃciently high to produce damage of
the insulation were small in relation to the total surface area of the elements. With these considerations,
afer performing all analysis, a signiﬁcant reduction of the ﬁre resistance was concluded.
Te considered local damages on the beam and columns caused a certain reduction of the ﬁre resistance.
In order to be able to compare the results of this study with previous ones on insulated steel frames an
equivalent uniform damage level was calculated for both beam and column. Tese equivalent uniform
insulation levels were derived from the results of the performed analysis and represent a uniform damage
level of the insulation over the entire element which would produce the same reduction of ﬁre resistance
as the local damage considered in this study. Te equivalent uniform insulation damage and the reduction
of ﬁre resistance caused by it are shown in Table 4.14.
Table 4.14: Member ﬁre resistance comparison with reference study

Te surface of damaged insulation considered in this study was 10% of the total surface area of the
beam, while this ratio was even lower, 4%, for the column. Te results showed that an equivalent uniform
insulation damage of 60% on the beam would produce the same reduction of post-earthquake ﬁre resis-
tance as the reduction found due to local insulation damage. Te equivalent uniform insulation damage
for the column was found to be even higher, 84%. Te numbers indicate, that the considerations of uniform
insulation damage levels used in previous studies underestimate the reduction of post-earthquake ﬁre re-
sistance. Tis is due to the fact that these studies disregard the fact that local damages of ﬁre insulation
occur exactly in the most solicited locations along the elements.
Conclusions of all the discussed cases were summarised in the following Chapter.

==PAGE:93==
Chapter 5
Conclusions and Discussions
5.1
Summary of Work
Te aim of this paper was to investigate the inﬂuence of an earthquake on the ﬁre resistance of the
structure, with particular focus on damage modelling of passive spray applied ﬁre protection aﬀected by
an earthquake.
A ﬁve storey, three bay, moment-resistant steel frame was designed according to current European
building codes, considering a seismic area in Italy. Te structural elements were made of standard I and
H proﬁles, while their material was modelled as a bi-linear elastic - perfectly plastic model. Te structure
was insulated with spray applied ﬁre insulation to ensure a ﬁre resistance over 60 minutes.
Tis structure was subjected to a sequential analysis. First a general gravity analysis was done on the
structure and was conﬁrmed that all elements have remained in the elastic region. Te gravity analysis
was followed by a seismic time history analysis. Seven diﬀerent earthquake motions were investigated in
a separate analysis. For each of these earthquakes the level of insulation damage was determined based
on local deformations of the structural elements. Te insulation damage criteria was based on a previous
study made by Braxtan et al., 2011 [9] and was considered to occur in locations where strain at the external
surface of the members reached 3ϵy. Fire insulation was considered to be fully damaged in the damaged
areas and to remain intact in the rest of the structure.
Two earthquakes were selected for further investigations: one producing the highest damage levels
and another one with an average response. Heat transfer analysis was performed for these cases in or-
der to model the temperature transition zones between the insulated and damaged parts of the beam and
column. Te results of the heat transfer analysis are represented by a time dependent temperature distri-
bution along the structural elements.
Finally, several ﬁre analysis were performed, and the time to failure of the structural elements and
global collapse was investigated. Te ﬁre was applied to the structural elements as a thermal load, for
which the temperature histories were found in the heat transfer model. Te most critical ﬁre scenario was
investigated in all post-earthquake ﬁre analysis. A total 7 ﬁre analysis were run on insulated steel frames
and 2 additional analysis were completed on non-insulated frames.
Te results of the sequential analysis were presented in Section 4. Te conclusions of the study are
described in the following part.
88
==PAGE:94==
Conclusions and Discussions - Conclusions
Fall Semester - 2019
5.2
Conclusions
Firstly, the results of the present study conﬁrm the ﬁndings of several previous studies made on non-
insulated steel frames, i.e. the second order eﬀects caused by the permanent deformations afer an earth-
quake have a very minor eﬀect on the ﬁre resistance of the structure and no signiﬁcant eﬀect on the
collapse mechanism.
Te veriﬁcation concluded that in case of non-insulated steel frames, the time until formation of three
plastic hinges in the beam is reduced by 0.22%, while the time until reaching the ultimate strain at the end
of the beam is reduced by 1.14%. Similarly, the time until failure of the column in form of buckling was
reduced by 1.23%-2.21%. Tis indicates a minor eﬀect caused by the ground motions.
Te conclusions were identical also for the case when the ﬁre resistance of the insulated steel frame was
compared before and afer the earthquake, without considerations of any damage of the ﬁre insulation.
Te reduction of beam and column failure time was between 0.22% and 1.50%.
Contrarily, the local damage of the beam insulation caused by the motion of the structure during earth-
quake events, has a major eﬀect on the ﬁre resistance of the beam, but does not aﬀect the global collapse.
During earthquake, plastic hinges are allowed to occur at the ends of the beams and at the botom of the
ground ﬂoor columns. Insulation is expected to get damaged in these zones (if earthquake is strong enough
to produce local strains over the considered limit). At the location of damaged insulation, higher temper-
atures will act than on the rest of the beam. Tese locations coincide with the critical sections, which are
monitored during ﬁre analysis. Te result is that the ﬁre resistance of the beam is drastically reduced, as
the beam ends get exposed to higher temperatures and therefore the plastic hinges close to the supports
form much faster during the ﬁre compared to when the insulation was not damaged due to a previous
earthquake.
It is important to note that even a small damage of the beam insulation (18 cm of damage considered
for the average earthquake, while 30 for the most demanding one) has a huge eﬀect on the ﬁre resistance.
Tis is due to the fact that the insulation damage of beam occurs in the areas where the plastic hinges
would form in a ﬁre only case. As an eﬀect, the material degrades faster in the areas of plastic hinges
and therefore reduces the ﬁre resistance of the beam. Tis is proven by the fact that the reduction of time
until the formation of the ﬁrst two plastic hinges is similar in case of an average earthquake (time reduced
by 73.64%) and most devastating earthquake (time reduced by 74.15%). Tis eﬀect leads to reaching the
ultimate strain (20%) at the beam ends much earlier. Te ultimate strain was not reached during the case
without earthquake as the global collapse happened earlier (afer 78 minutes), while it has reached ultimate
values afer around 47 minutes when the beam insulation has experienced some damage afer earthquake.
On the other hand, neither the behaviour of the columns nor the global mechanism was aﬀected by the
early failure of the beam (failure of the beam was considered when strain reached 20% at the plastic hinge
near supports). In this case global collapse was reached by symmetrical collapse of the structure due to
the loss of two columns.
Another highlight is that the damage of the insulation at the botom of the ground ﬂoor columns has
a great eﬀect on both the time and mechanism of global collapse. Results show that the time until the
columns buckled was reduced by 50% and the time until reaching ultimate strain was reduced by 54% in
the case in which the column insulation experienced damage. Te damage on the column insulation was
located at the base of the columns and was considered over a length of 18 cm, while the total column length
is 4.5 m. Tis shows that even if the insulation damage is local over a short area, the eﬀect is big.
Damage of the ﬁre insulation of the column changes the global collapse mechanism. In all previous cases
failure has occurred by the structures collapse on itself as a result of losing two columns due to exces-
sive deformations. In the case of the damaged column insulation, the column has failed due to excessive
compressive forces in the damaged area. Furthermore, the global collapse occurred by deformation of the
structure in the direction of the permanent deformations caused by the earthquake and ﬁnally collapsing
89
==PAGE:95==
Conclusions and Discussions - Conclusions
Fall Semester - 2019
on itself. Buildings in seismic zones are designed in such way that plastic hinge formation is allowed at
the base of the ground ﬂoor columns. In order to experience such damage of insulation it is required to
have earthquakes which are strong enough to trigger the formation of these plastic hinges and to cause
deformations above the insulation damage limit criteria.
A comparison with previous studies made on insulated frames showed the importance of understand-
ing the paterns of local insulation damage. All of the known previous studies, e.g. [6] on insulated steel
frames were based on a uniform level of ﬁre insulation damage. Te uniform insulation damage levels were
applied in these studies by uniformly reducing the thermal resistance of the insulation material over the
entire length of the structural members. Post-earthquake ﬁre analysis performed based on these consid-
erations showed that a signiﬁcant reduction of the beam ﬁre resistance occurs only at uniform insulation
damage over 25%. Signiﬁcant reduction of ﬁre resistance occurred earlier in case of the column. Te area
of local insulation damage considered in this study was 10% of the total surface area of the beam, while this
ratio was even lower, 4% for the column. An equivalent uniform insulation damage was calculated based
on the ﬁre resistance reduction found from the performed PEF analysis. Te results showed that an equiv-
alent uniform insulation damage of 60% for the beam would cause the same reduction of ﬁre resistance as
the reduction found due to the local insulation damage. Tis ratio was even higher for the column, 84%,
while in reality only 4% of the beam and 10% of the column insulation was damaged. Tese results indicate
the importance of insulation damage modelling, and show that considerations of uniform insulation dam-
age levels used in previous studies underestimate the reduction of post-earthquake ﬁre resistance. Tis is
due to the fact that these studies disregard the fact that local damages of ﬁre insulation occur exactly in
the most solicited locations along the elements.
Te above conclusions indicate that both the ﬁre resistance of the element and the collapse of the
building may be strongly aﬀected by the occurrence of a previous earthquake, due to the local damages
of the ﬁre protection at sensitive locations. In order to maintain the design ﬁre resistance, the integrity
of the insulation during an earthquake must be preserved or, alternatively, an advanced structural design
must be carried out, where the concurrent occurrence of earthquake and ﬁre is considered. In both cases,
additional experimental studies are deemed necessary, in order to assess the sensitivity of the diﬀerent
protective materials to local stresses and strains and evaluate or limit the insulation loss or damage during
earthquakes.
All the above remarks were made for structures designed to current seismic and ﬁre codes, with adher-
ence to both ultimate and serviceability limit states. Te SLS design condition imposes maximum storey
deformations, which can be met only (for moment resistant frames) with increased size of columns (com-
pared to the minimum necessary in ULS). Tis means that during ﬁre, there is a considerable amount of
surplus capacity due to the increased size of columns. Buildings designed based on older seismic codes,
without storey-drif limitations have higher utilisation ratios in regards to vertical forces, which means
that the above listed eﬀects of pre-ﬁre earthquakes will be stronger for such buildings.
It has been proven that second-order eﬀects due to the permanent deformation afer an earthquake
have no important eﬀect on the ﬁre resistance of the structure, while local damage of the ﬁre protective
coating reduces the ﬁre resistance signiﬁcantly. Terefore, insulation damage modelling is of key impor-
tance for ﬁnding the correct structural response. Despite this, there is very litle information available
regarding the adherence of spray applied protective materials or intumescent paint to structural steel at
high deformations. Tere are numerous products on the market but it is very hard to ﬁnd out at which
strains of the structural element are these protective layers expected to crack or delaminate. A very lim-
ited number of studies have focused on this area. As seen, local insulation damage can have huge eﬀects
on the ﬁre resistance, therefore it is necessary to conduct detailed testing to understand adherence under
dynamic loading and excessive strains.
90
==PAGE:96==
Conclusions and Discussions - Limits of Validity and Further Studies
Fall Semester - 2019
A ﬁnal comment is related to the performance of the OpenSees sofware. Although some limitations
have been encountered with the thermo-mechanical material models, the sofware oﬀers the user a huge
amount of freedom and ﬂexibility to build FEM codes and has incorporated a great variety of powerful FEM
methods. Te possibility of using Tcl programming language in the implementation of the FEM script gives
the beneﬁt of optimisation of the use of the solver algorithms and therefore reduce computational time.
In reference to a previous study made by [5], the computational time was reduced by 5 times, with the
duration of one single analysis to be around 2 hours. Te limitation of the thermo-mechanical model refers
to the available material models. A simpliﬁed elastic-perfectly plastic material model had to be used due
to errors found in the other suitable material models (related to plastic behaviour, an issue communicated
to the developers of OpenSees thermal module).
5.3
Limits of Validity and Further Studies
Tere are a few limitations of the study which are necessary to consider. Tese are listed below:
• Te thesis focused on moment-resistant steel frames only. Other type of structures were not con-
sidered in the analysis.
• Te analysis were done on 2D FEM models.
Te unfavourable eﬀects of out-of plane buckling of columns was not modelled. Te columns were
oriented with the weak axis out of plane, and therefore they have buckled when the loads reached
critical values in the strong axis. Tis meant that the collapse times are overestimated. Considering
out of plane buckling might also change the collapse mechanism in case of the analysis in which
beam failure happened before the failure of the columns.
2D modelling is not an issue for the beams as in reality the concrete slabs have a stabilising eﬀect on
the beams, thus they prevent out of plane buckling of the beams. Modelling of cold concrete slabs
would require extra computational time, while modelling of heated concrete slabs is a complex issue
on its own. Terefore 2D modelling of the beam is a suﬃcient, accepted practice.
As all analysis were done on 2D models, the obtained relative reductions of ﬁre resistance afer the
post-earthquake ﬁre are expected to be valid even in a 3D model where out-of plane buckling of
columns would happen before in plane buckling. In contrast, the collapse mechanism might change
due to the earlier out of plane buckling of the columns.
• Earthquake motions were considered in the primary direction of the 2D model and the eﬀects of the
earthquake on the second direction were disregarded.
• Connection of the beam-column joint was not investigated.
Tere are a few areas in which further development could be made to complete the present study.
Tese will be discussed in the following.
3D Model
Te present study focused on a simpliﬁed 2D frame, which is suﬃcient to observe the general collapse
behaviour and to monitor most collapse mechanism. Despite this, one important mechanism which can
not be recorded by a 2D study is the out of plane buckling of the column which is a real risk as the stiﬀness
of the columns in the out of plane direction is lower than in plane of the frame. Furthermore, a 3D analysis
has an eﬀect on the earthquake response as currently ground motions in one directions were considered,
while in reality there is a contribution to the earthquake forces of at least 30% from the second direction.
Accounting for such eﬀects among other could induce torsion in the structure.
91
==PAGE:97==
Conclusions and Discussions - Limits of Validity and Further Studies
Fall Semester - 2019
Bond testing of protective coating
Te amount of information know about spray applied ﬁre resistant materials and intumescent paint is
limited. Laboratory testing of the adherence of the protective coating on the structural steel under dynamic
loading could provide essential information for understanding the potential insulation damage paterns.
Such testing is planned to be conducted in the following semesters at DTU.
Post-earthquake ﬁre analysis
It would be relevant to study non-moment resistant steel frames with braces as well. In general the
columns of moment resistant frames have a signiﬁcant over-strength in respect to lateral loads, therefore
it would be interesting to investigate structures with less over-strength and higher utilisation ratio as this
would have an eﬀect on the time until collapse.
Reﬁned temperature distribution
Similarly to this study and to previous studies, ﬁre has been applied as an average temperature on
the structural elements. In reality, as shown in Section C.5 and 4.4, the temperature distribution along
the cross-sections is not uniform. Furthermore, the inﬂuence of the unprotected beam end as a result of
shaking during earthquakes was considered to be small on the temperature development of the columns
and therefore this eﬀect was disregarded. Tis inﬂuence was brieﬂy looked at in Section 4.4 and could be
further investigated with more reﬁned 3D models.
Material model
Te basis of the current study was a temperature dependent bi-linear perfectly elasto-plastic material
model. Te model has been chosen due to the current limitations of OpenSees sofware, but overestimates
the strength and stiﬀness of the material in the elastic-plastic transition zone. A more appropriate model,
with smooth, curved transition between the elastic and plastic regions could provide more realistic results.
Te results found by this study are a good basis, as the eﬀect of the earthquake on the post-earthquake
ﬁre resistance can be well established, with the only diﬀerence being an overestimation of the time until
failure.
Fire spread
Te study was limited to one main ﬁre scenario in the central bay at the botom ﬂoor. Te eﬀect of
diﬀerent position (e.g. side bay or upper ﬂoor) or extension of the ﬁre (e.g, ﬁre spread in the horizontal or
vertical directions) could be usefully investigated in the future.

Appendix A
Appendix A - Handbook of OpenSees
OpenSees is an open source object oriented sofware framework for simulation applications in struc-
tural, geotechnical, earthquake and ﬁre engineering. Te sofware is based on the tcl coding language and
in it’s original form does not have a pre- and post-processor.
OpenSees is a powerful tool for analysing non-linear systems using numerical methods. It has a large
built in library of materials, element types and equation solvers. Te sofware is modular and open source,
meaning that the users are allowed to create their own materials, elements and sections (by correctly deﬁn-
ing force-deformation, moment-curvature and material stress-strain models for them).
Te core structure of the OpenSees analysis is illustrated in Figure A.1. Any code is made up of four
main parts:
• ModelBuilder - Creates the objects of the analysis (materials, elements, nodes, constraints, loads,
time-series,etc.) and adds them to the domain
• Domain - Stores all information about the created objects in each time ti. Provides these information
to the Recorder and Analysis
• Analysis - Performs all calculations necessary to move the state of the model from time ti to ti + dt
• Recorder - Monitors and records parameters during the analysis
Figure A.1: OpenSees structure
In the following the most important parts of the sofware will be described.
A.1
ModelBuilder and Domain
Te ModelBuilder is responsible for creating and adding to the domain the following objects: nodes,
masses, materials, sections, elements, load paterns, time series, transformation types, blocks and con-
straints. Te ﬁrst input of the ModelBuilder should be a command describing the dimensions of the prob-
lem (1D, 2D, 3D) and the number of degrees of freedom at each node.
96
==PAGE:102==
Appendix A - Handbook of OpenSees
Fall Semester - 2019
ModelBuilder is only used to create all this information in the ﬁrst step, but all information about these
objects get stored in the Domain during the analysis. Terefore, the objects of the domain can be seen in
Figure A.2.
Figure A.2: OpenSees domain classes
Some of critical objects in the current study are described further on.
A.1.1
Materials - Sections - Elements
Tere are more than 100 diﬀerent types of material models currently available in the library of OpenSees,
including several type of elastic, elasto-plastic, hardening, concrete, steel, hysteretic, viscous, soil-structure
interaction materials, etc.
Materials are deﬁned to describe the stress-strain relationship in the model.
Sections are used to deﬁne moment-curvature model at speciﬁc cross sections of the beam-column
element. In general three types of sections can be used: elastic, resultant and ﬁber. For the current study
the most relevant section type is the ﬁber section. General ﬁber sections are decomposed into sub-regions
of simpler, regular shaped parts called patches or ﬁbers. Analysis responses in terms of stress and strain
results can be queried for any of the ﬁbers deﬁned for the cross-sections.
Elements on the other hand, are used to deﬁne the force-deformation model. Tey can be truss, zero-
length, beam-column, joint, bearing contact, cable and many other type of elements. As the current study
is focusing on frames, the most relevant type of elements are of the beam-column type. Elastic, elastic
Timoshenko, and diﬀerent type of lumped and distributed plasticity elements.
For distributed plasticity problems displacement and force based beam-column elements can be used. Te
advantage of spread plasticity elements is that yielding is allowed to occur at any location along the ele-
ment. Tis is important for structures with distributed element loads.
Displacement Based Elements
Tese elements are based on the standard ﬁnite element formulation in which an approximate dis-
placement ﬁeld is used to interpolate section deformations. Te principle of virtual displacements is then
used to establish the equilibrium equations.
For this element type constant axial deformation and linearly distributed curvature is considered along
the element.
Due to the linear approximation of the curvature, a mesh reﬁnement is necessary to produce accurate
results for higher order deformation distributions.
97
==PAGE:103==
Appendix A - Handbook of OpenSees
Fall Semester - 2019
Force Based Elements
Force based elements are built on the availability of exact equilibrium solutions within the basic beam-
column element system. In such formulation, the equilibrium between element and section forces is exact.
Section forces are found by interpolating of the basic forces in the basic system from static equilibrium.
Terefore, constant axial force and linear bending moment distribution (if no distributed loads act on the
element) are considered.
Beams and columns of a frame structure are deﬁned on three levels in the model. First an element type
is chosen for which a section is deﬁned having pre-deﬁned material properties. Te scheme can be seen
in Figure A.3.
Figure A.3: OpenSees Section Deﬁnition
Springs are modelled using zero-length elements with stiﬀness considered in form of material proper-
ties in each of the degrees of freedom.
98
==PAGE:104==
Appendix A - Handbook of OpenSees
Fall Semester - 2019
A.1.2
Geometric Transformations
Elements have one more important property which needs to be deﬁned in the domain, called the
geometric transformation. Te three available geometric transformations are Linear, PDelta and Coro-
tational and they have a signiﬁcant eﬀect on the analysis results as they describe how to account for
geometrical non-linearity in the model.
Te geometric transformation is used to transform forces and stiﬀness matrices between the local and
global coordinate systems. Te geometric transformation function is called for all elements at every single
step when incremental displacements are computed. Te procedure given by [31] is as follows:
• 1 - Te element receives global displacements from the connected nodes.
• 2 - Te element send the global displacements to the geometric transformation.
• 3 - Te element receives natural displacements from the geometric transformation.
• 4 - Te element along with any constitutive relations (sections, etc.) compute element forces and
stiﬀnesses in the natural coordinates.
• 5 - Te element sends the element forces and stiﬀness in the natural coordinates to the geometric
transformation.
• 6 - Te element receives element forces and stiﬀnesses in global coordinates from the geometric
transformation.
• 7 - Te element sends (or rather makes available to send upon request) the element forces and stiﬀ-
ness in global coordinates to the analysis.
Te characteristics of the three available geometric transformations are [32]:
• Linear - It transforms stiﬀnesses and forces linearly from basic to global coordinate system.
• PDelta - Performs the same transformation as above, but it also considers second order P-Delta
eﬀects.
• Corotational - Performs an exact geometric transformation.
A.1.3
Loads
Basically four type of load paterns can be applied to a model. Tese are plain patern, uniform excita-
tion, multi support excitation patern and temperature paterns.
Plain paterns include all load deﬁnitions including point and distributed loads while earthquake and
temperature loads can be deﬁned from external data ﬁles including time dependent variation of the load
property.
99
==PAGE:105==
Appendix A - Handbook of OpenSees
Fall Semester - 2019
A.2
Analysis
Tis object is responsible for performing the analysis. In order to run any analysis all the sub-objects
seen in Figure A.4 have to be described.
Figure A.4: OpenSees analysis classes
Te diﬀerent sub-objects were explained based on the OpenSees command manual [32].
A.2.1
ConstraintHandler
It determines how boundary conditions and imposed displacements are handled in case constraints
are used.
A.2.2
DOF Numberer
Has the simple purpose of determining the mapping between the degrees-of-freedom and equation
numbers. Te two main type of numberers are Plain, in which the nodes are arbitrary assigned degrees-
of-freedom based on the input ﬁle and RC in which the degrees-of-freedom are assigned based on the
Reversed Cuthill-McKee algorithm.
A.2.3
AnalysisModel
Determines the type of analysis to be performed. Te three options are static, transient and variable
transient analysis.
A.2.4
Integrator
Determines the base for prediction of the next step in an analysis, speciﬁes the tangent stiﬀness ma-
trix and residual vector for each iteration and determines the corrective step based on the displacement
increment dU.
Static and transient analysis types have diﬀerent integrators. Te main intregation types for static
analysis are:
• Load Control : λn = λn−1 + dλ
• Displacement Control : Ujn = Ujn−1 + dUj
For transient analysis Newmark or Hilbert-Hughes-Taylor integrator methods can be used.
A.2.5
SolutionAlgorithm
Determines the sequence of steps to be taken to solve the non-linear equations at the current step.
100
==PAGE:106==
Appendix A - Handbook of OpenSees
Fall Semester - 2019
A.2.5.1
Algorithm
Linear, Newton, Newton with Line Search, Modiﬁed Newton, Krylov-Newton, BFGS and Broyden type
of algorithms can be chosen.
Te preferred one is the Newton algorithm which is based on the Newton-Raphson method.
Te following equilibrium equation can be writen in the ﬁnite element framework:
[K]{u} = {F a}
(A.1)
Where [K] is the coeﬃcient matrix, u is the vector of unknown degrees of freedom and F a is the
vector of applied loads. Te equations are nonlinear if the coeﬃcient matrix is a function of the degrees of
freedom. Te Newton-Raphson method provides an iterative process to solve these equations. Te method
is based on Equation A.2.
{∆ui} = {F a} −{F nr
i }
{ui+1} = {ui} + {∆ui}
(A.2)
Where, i is an index referring to the current step of iteration, i+1 is the next iteration step, [Kt
i] is the
tangent stiﬀness matrix at the current step and {F nr
i } is the restoring force vector based on the element
stresses.
In each step, [Kt
i] and {F nr
i } are evaluated based on the current displacement vector {ui}. Figure A.5
represents a general step of the Newton-Raphson iteration method.
Figure A.5: General step of simple Newton-Raphson iteration
Usually one step is not suﬃcient to fulﬁl convergence criteria, therefore the following iterative algo-
rithm is followed:
• 1. Assume {ui} as 0 for the ﬁrst step and the converged solution of the previous time step for every
other iteration.
• 2. Using the assumed {ui} calculate the [Kt
i] and {F nr
i }
• 3. Generate {∆ui} using Equation A.2 and then ﬁnd {ui+1}
• 4. Repeat step 2 and 3 until convergence criteria is achieved.
101
==PAGE:107==
Appendix A - Handbook of OpenSees
Fall Semester - 2019
Figure A.6: Final step of simple Newton-Raphson iteration
Te above described algorithm provides a solution which is in equilibrium for the load level {F a},
therefore the equation {F nr
i } = {F a} is true within tolerance. Ofen, analysis can not be performed
for one single load level. Such case is for example involving plasticity when the response is not linear,
therefore it is necessary to ﬁnd an iterative way of increasing the load level until the ﬁnal load level is
reached. Following such procedure will mean, that the solution of the intermediate steps also have to be
in equilibrium. Tis is possible by performing separate Netwon-Raphson iterations for each loading step.
Equation A.3 presents the iteration for a general load increment.
[Kt
n,i]{∆ui} = {F a
n} −{F nr
n,i}
(A.3)
Where the parameter n refers to the loading step n. Te incremental Newton-Raphson procedure is
shown in Figure A.7.
Figure A.7: Incremental Newton-Raphson iteration
Te incremental procedure has the beneﬁt that the solution at every single load increment iteration
has to be closed to the exact solution.
Te above described procedure is the full Newton-Raphson algorithm as it calculates the stiﬀness ma-
trix at every iteration. Calculating the stiﬀness matrix at each step is a time consuming calculation re-
quiring strong computational power for complex systems. For this reason an alternative method called
102
==PAGE:108==
Appendix A - Handbook of OpenSees
Fall Semester - 2019
Newton with initial stiﬀness was developed, in which the stiﬀness matrix is calculated in the ﬁrst step
only and used through all iterations. Tis method converges slower than the original method, as seen in
Figure A.8, but still has a shorter total analysis time as the time consuming calculation of the stiﬀness
matrix is skipped at each step.
Figure A.8: Newton-Raphson with initial stiﬀness
A.2.5.2
Convergence Test
A convergence test is necessary to deﬁne if convergence has been achieved by the algorithm at the
end of the iteration step. Te convergence test is applied to the following equation:
K · ∆U = R
(A.4)
Te user deﬁnes the magnitude of the tolerance to be considered for the iteration steps and a test
method has to be selected considering the equation which they fulﬁl:
• Norm Unbalance :
√
RR < tol
• Norm Displacement Increment :
√
∆UT · ∆U < tol
• Energy Increment : 1
2(∆UT · R) < tol
A.2.6
SystemofEqn / Solver
Determines how to store and solve the system of equations within the solution algorithm. Te preferred
option is BandGeneral SOE which constructs an un-symmetric banded system of equations which will
be factored and solved during the analysis using the Lapack band general solver[32].
103
==PAGE:109==
Appendix B
Appendix B - Structural Design of Case
Study Building
As mentioned in the previous chapter, the purpose of this study was to investigate the ﬁre insulation
damage during earthquakes. For this reason, the intention was to use the same frame as the one used in
the case study of an ofen referenced study in the topic of PEF, [11]. In this study the frame was design
based on a previous version of Eurocode, with considering only ultimate limit states. A later study, [5] re-
designed the structure in such way to meet current standard ultimate and serviceability limit states. Te
results of the later study in terms of storey deformations were somewhat lower than the expected values,
therefore it has been decided to re-verify the structure to see if further optimisation can be made. Tis
was necessary, as Eurocode already provides demanding damage limitations for earthquake events and in
a cost driven, real life design it is highly improbable to choose bigger elements than the ones necessary to
meet the design criteria.
Te design of the case study structure was based on the Eurocode. Te following codes were consid-
ered:
• [1] for general rules, loads and combinations
• [7] for steel and ﬁre design
• [8] for seismic design
• [27] for steel temperature dependent material properties
B.1
Design Criteria
Eurocode considers diﬀerent limit states, beyond which the structure can no longer fulﬁl the relevant
design criteria.
B.1.1
Steel Member Ultimate and Serviceability Limit States
Ultimate limit states (ULS) correspond collapse or similar forms of failure and generally refer to the
maximum load-carrying capacity of the structure and its members. Serviceability limit states (SLS) on the
other hand are states beyond which certain service requirements for structures and their members can not
be met.
Member and global structural veriﬁcation within these limit states were performed by the built-in
code check function of Robot 2018. As part of these veriﬁcation, the following member parameters were
checked: cross-sectional class, axial, shear and bending capacity, stability in terms of slenderness, buckling
and lateral-torsional buckling as well as maximum deformations.
104
==PAGE:110==
Appendix B - Structural Design of Case Study Building
Fall Semester - 2019
B.1.2
Seismic Ultimate and Serviceability Limit States
In case of seismic design it should be veriﬁed that the whole structure and the individual members
have suﬃcient ductility in accordance with the selected system and its expected ductility. It is desired to
eliminate completely the risk of britle failure therefore additional material related requirements have to
be met.
An important goal of the Eurocode seismic design is to provide structure with strong columns and
weaker beams, so failures due to excessive seismic forces appear ﬁrst in beams instead of columns. Fur-
thermore, sof storey plastic mechanism are to be prevented.
Te most important design criteria are described below.
B.1.2.1
Strong Column - Weak Beam Condition
For every joint the following condition should be met:
X
MRc >= 1.3
X
MRb
(B.1)
Where, MRc is the sum of the design moment resistance of columns framing the joint MRb is the sum
of design moment resistance of the beams framing the joint
B.1.2.2
Inter-storey Drif
Te design inter-storey drif, dr is deﬁned as the diﬀerence between the average lateral displacements
ds at the top and botom of the storey, while ds can be found as:
ds = qd · de
(B.2)
Where qd is the behaviour factor depending on the structure and design ductility level and de is the
displacement of the structure at the same point under the design seismic spectra.
Tree diﬀerent criteria are given for the inter-storey drif, depending on the type of non-structural
elements of the building. Te criteria are
drµ <= 0.005h
(B.3)
-for structures with britle non-structural elements atached to the structure
drµ <= 0.0075h
(B.4)
-for structures with ductile non-structural elements atached to the structure
drµ <= 0.010h
(B.5)
-for structures with non-structural elements which are completely independent of the structure and its
deformations during earthquakes.
µ is the reduction factor depending on the importance class and h is the storey height.
In addition, second order (P −∆) eﬀects has to be considered in the analysis if θ, the inter-storey drif
sensitivity coeﬃcient is lower than 0.1.
θ = Ptot · dr
Vtot · h <= 0.1
(B.6)
Where, Ptot is the tototal gravity load at and above the storey and Vtot is the total seismic storey shear.

Appendix C
Appendix C - Benchmarks
Te benchmarks have the purpose of validating the work performed in this thesis. A series of bench-
mark exercises were performed to assess the behaviour of steel material, structural stability, response to
earthquake actions and response on elevated temperature. Te results obtained from OpenSees sofware
were compared against analytical solutions and results obtained by other sofwares. Results obtained by
diﬀerent methods, which were within a certain acceptance limit validate the work performed and show
that the initial assumptions and problem deﬁnition of the benchmark exercises were correctly introduced
and analysed in the chosen sofware.
C.1
Nonlinear Material - Plasticity Benchmark
C.1.1
Double Fixed Beam Under Point Load
Ofen structural design of elements is made using linear elastic theories due to their simplicity. How-
ever, plastic behaviour of materials allow the elements to experience higher stress-strain states above the
elastic capacities. Te additional capacity of the material plastic region is a crucial consideration in ﬁre
and earthquake design, therefore it is necessary to investigate it in the present project.
In an elastic material, the stress distribution in any section is linear. In case of a plastic material
the stress distribution is linear as long as the section stresses are lower than the yielding value. Stress
distribution becomes non-linear when loads further increase, causing stresses above the yielding limit.
From this point the material enters in the plastic region and experiences irreversible deformations. Stresses
get redistributed in the areas where the material starts to yield. Plastic hinges form in the areas where the
sectional forces increase above the plastic limits. Further increment of forces cause stress redistribution
until further plastic hinges appear, eventually leading to a failure mechanism. Te number of plastic hinges
which can form in an element depends on the degree of static indeterminacy.
To validate the plasticity model, the ﬁrst benchmark focused on the behaviour of a simple element sub-
jected to increasing point load. A double clamped steel beam, with rectangular steel section was analysed
under increasing point load. Te structural problem is illustrated in Figure C.1.
Figure C.1: Plasticity Benchmark - Beam under punctual load and clamped at both ends
112
==PAGE:118==
Appendix C - Benchmarks
Fall Semester - 2019
Table C.1 summarises the information regarding the used section and material.
Table C.1: Plasticity benchmark - Point load problem properties

Poisson’s Ratio

C.1.1.1

As a ﬁrst exercises the beam was loaded to the elastic limit. Te elastic moment distribution under an

in point A. Terefore the analysis was done for a load which produces a moment in point A equal to the
elastic capacity. Te elastic moment capacity is given by Equation C.1.
Mel = fy · Wel,y = 4437.5kNm
(C.1)
Te moment in point A is given by Equation C.2.
Mel,A = Pe · 0.2 · 0.520.72
(C.2)
