C.1.1.2
Plastic Behaviour
Design of structures are ofen performed entirely in the elastic region, without considering any capac-
ity above the elastic limit. In case of accidental actions, like earthquake and ﬁre, the plastic reserves and
ultimate resistance of the structure is of interest. Terefore, the benchmark was continued to analyse and
validate the non-linear behaviour of the steel material. Te above ﬁrst benchmark was used as a basis of
the exercise, in which the load was increased until a mechanism formed.
Understanding the plastic behaviour with accurate prediction of the plastic region is a complicated
calculation to perform by hand. A number of assumptions were made about the material behaviour in
order to be able to use a simpliﬁed hand calculation method:
• Plasticity is formed only at plastic hinges which have zero length
• Te eﬀect of strain hardening is ignored (for sofware calculations an insigniﬁcantly small value of
10−6 was considered)
• Te element is considered to be strong enough to avoid local and lateral-torsional buckling
• Te plastic hinges can be subjected to inﬁnite plastic deformations
• Hypothesis of small displacements is applied, therefore no geometrical non-linearity considered
With the above assumptions a relation between the increasing load and deﬂection of the beam can be
done. Te load-deﬂection ratio is assessed in a series of steps. First the moment distribution under elastic
load was found as shown in Figure C.3. Te load was increased until the ﬁrst plastic moment was reached
in point A. A plastic hinge forms and plastic rotations develop. Te plastic hinge allows free rotation
therefore in the second step of the analysis a beam with modiﬁed static scheme was modelled.
Figure C.3: Plasticity Benchmark - Beam under punctual load - Plastic moment distribution
At the end of the ﬁrst step the loads, deﬂections were recorded and a reserve of 0.57Mpl and 0.40Mpl
is lef until the plastic moment is achieved in point B and C.
114
==PAGE:120==
Appendix C - Benchmarks
Fall Semester - 2019
In step two the clamped support from point A is changed to a simple support. Another analysis is
performed until the next plastic hinge is formed. Te load is increased until a value at which the produced
bending moment reaches the value of the plastic moment reserve found in the previous step. Te load
and deﬂection was found by adding the results together from step one and two. At the end of this step a
reserve of 0.26Mpl was found in point C.
Te third step was performed similarly. Te plastic hinge from the second step was modelled in the
static scheme as a hinge in point B. Te load was increased until the reserve of 0.26Mpl was reached in
point C. Te ﬁnal loads and deﬂection were found by superimposing the results from the three steps.
Afer the third step, three plastic hinges formed in the structure and therefore a mechanism has been
formed. Increasing the load further would mean inﬁnite deformations.
Figure C.4 shows the results of the step-by-step hand calculations. Clear change in the structural
stiﬀness can be seen as a consequence of the plastic hinge developments.
Figure C.4: Plasticity benchmark - Hand calculations results
OpenSees ﬁnite element sofware was used to model the above problem. An elasto-plastic material
with a very small hardening ratio of 10−6 was used. A static non-linear analysis was performed using two
procedures: load and displacement imposed. In the ﬁrst case, an ultimate load and number of steps was
deﬁned for which the program imposed a series of load steps until the ultimate load was reached. In the
second procedure displacements were imposed at a given node. An expected ﬁnal displacement was given
for which the sofware imposed a series of displacements. Te results of the two methods are shown on
Figure C.5. Te state of sectional stresses are shown in the same ﬁgure for diﬀerent stages of the response,
for three characteristic points along the beam (two ends and mid-span).
115
==PAGE:121==
Appendix C - Benchmarks
Fall Semester - 2019
Figure C.5: Plasticity benchmark - OpenSees results
Te load and displacement imposed sofware results together with the solution of the hand calculations
are shown in Figure C.6.
Figure C.6: Load-Displacement curve for beam under punctual load and clamped at both ends
116
==PAGE:122==
Appendix C - Benchmarks
Fall Semester - 2019
It can be noticed that the diﬀerences between all three results are really small for both force and
deformations. A diﬀerence between the methods of under 2% was obtained for the ultimate force. For
this simple problem no signiﬁcant diﬀerence can be noticed between the force and displacement imposed
solutions.
Te used non-linear static analysis provides spread plasticity solution for which it is not possible to
deﬁne an exact force-deformation value at which the plastic hinge forms (like in the assumption of the
hand calculations). Te deformations are allowed to continue until big displacements are achieved and it
is challenging to deﬁned the ultimate state.
Te results given by the sofware can be further calibrated as they depend on a series of aspect. Element
type, mesh size, number of section integration point and number of ﬁber integration points all inﬂuence
the ﬁnal results, therefore the eﬀect of each of these aspects are studied in Appendix D.
Another way to compare results is to compare the length of plastic hinge calculated by hand and given
by the program. Te length of the plastic hinge is deﬁned to be the distance between the location of plastic
and elastic moment near a plastic hinge.

C.2
Non-linear Geometry Benchmark
A structure is considered to be in equilibrium state if the structural response balances the applied loads.
In this state, small displacements appear if the load is increased. Structural instability is when these dis-
placements start to increase faster than the applied loads until a value which is too much for the current
loading conﬁguration. Tis occurs due to the imperfections of the structure and it is called buckling.
Te system is considered to be in trivial conﬁguration if the loads applied are lower than the smallest
value for which buckling happens. Tis speciﬁc value is called the critical load.
Euler problems are categorised by a speciﬁc type of buckling, in which a linear relationship between
the load and deformations is considered. Te increasing deformations deteriorate the structure’s stiﬀness
for deformations modes, which are primarily orthogonal to the trivial conﬁguration. Te stiﬀness de-
creases until the load reaches the critical value, when the stiﬀness goes to zero and the deformation mode
overlaps the trivial conﬁguration. Tis point is called a bifurcation in the equilibrium path and the load
afer this can not be further increased.
Te solution of such problems can be found using the second order theory as the equation of the po-
tential energy in the analytical solution takes a second order form.
Te ﬁrst order theory has the following hypotheses:
• Small displacements - According to which the equilibrium equations are writen for the non-deformed
shape of the structure and analytical simpliﬁcations can be made (sin(θ) = θ).
• Linear elastic materials
• Constant boundary conditions
In the second order theory, the equations are writen for the deformed shape of the structure, but an-
alytical simpliﬁcations can still be made.
It is critical to know the buckling behaviour of any member as buckling failure usually appears at mag-
nitudes of loading much lower than the ones the resistance of the material could support. In such cases
failure does not happen due to appearance of plastic hinges leading to mechanism, but due to instability
under the critical load.
Tree diﬀerent types of post buckling behaviour can distinguished if the assumption of small displace-
ments is disregarded. In such case the equilibrium equations are writen for the deformed shape without
using analytical simpliﬁcations. Te three types of post-buckling behaviour describe the load-deformation
development afer the critical load has been reached and are the following:
• Stable symmetric - Te load capacity increases with displacement increments
• Unstable symmetric - Te load capacity decreases with displacement increments
• Asymmetric - Te load capacity increase with displacement increment in one direction and decreases
in the other direction
Initial imperfections of the structure can further reduce the critical load at which buckling failure ap-
pears. Imperfections can have a beneﬁcial or disadvantageous eﬀect on the post-buckling behaviour of the
structure. Te structure is insensitive to imperfections if due to imperfections afer reaching the critical
load the element can support even higher loads, up until the material resistance. If the load capacity re-
duces afer the critical load, then the structure is called sensitive to imperfections.
121
==PAGE:127==
Appendix C - Benchmarks
Fall Semester - 2019
Te diﬀerent post-buckling behaviours were studied using OpenSees sofware. A simple problem with
a rigid rod (inﬁnite stiﬀness) and axial load was analysed in the program. Stable and unstable post-buckling
behaviours were generated by considering diﬀerent boundary conditions. Stable post-buckling behaviour
is expected in case of a rigid rod with rotational spring at the base, while unstable behaviour is expected
in case of similar rod, but with translational spring at the top. Te two systems studied are illustrated in
Figure C.12.
Figure C.12: Rigid rod systems with expected stable and unstable post-buckling behaviour

Te equilibrium of the rod can be writen for the deformed shape as:
P · L · sin(θ) = k · θ

(C.13)

C.2.3
Ideal Rigid Rod with Translational Spring at Top
Similarly to the previous case, the below system can also be described in relation to one single degree
of freedom, angle θ.
Figure C.16: Ideal rigid rod with translational spring at top
Te magnitude of spring force can be expressed as:
F = k · δ = k · L · sin(θ)
(C.19)
Te equilibrium equation can be writen in the deformed shape as:
P · L · sin(θ) = F · L · cos(θ) =⇒P · L · sin(θ) = k · L2 · sin(θ) cos(θ)
(C.20)
Considering the hypothesis of small displacement leads to:
P · θ = k · L · θ =⇒θ(P −k · L) = 0
(C.21)
Similarly to the previous ideal case, the equation has two solutions: the trivial solution and another solution
which is found by:
Pcr = k · L
(C.22)
Unstable post-buckling behaviour can be noticed if the hypothesis of large displacements is considered
instead. For such a case the two solutions are the trivial one and the one found by the below expression:
P = k · L · cos(θ)
(C.23)

C.2.5
Buckling of Cantilever Beam
Buckling behaviour was investigated in another practical example, in which a cantilever column was
loaded by axial load, see Figure C.19. Numerical analysis of such example ﬁnds the trivial solution unless
an imperfection is introduced in the system. If an ideal beam is loaded by a perfectly centric load, then
no second-order eﬀects appear and deformations continue in the form of contraction until the material’s
compressive strength is reached. In reality, ideal geometry and perfectly eccentric loading in structures is
hard to achieve and in most cases these imperfections are present. In the current problem, such imperfec-
tion is created by introducing a small moment at the end of the beam.
Figure C.19: Cantilever beam conﬁguration
Table C.6: Properties of cantilever beam subjected to axial loading
Area [cm2]
Beam length [cm]
Young’s Modulus [N/cm2]

==PAGE:134==
Appendix C - Benchmarks
Fall Semester - 2019
Te material and geometrical properties of the problem was chosen in such way to be able to compare
the results with analytical solutions given in [33]. Furthermore numerical results were presented for this
speciﬁc problem by [31]. His work focused on mathematical description and demonstration of the non-
linear geometry of beam-column elements in OpenSees.
Geometric non-linearity is handled in OpenSees by the geometric transformation class, presented in
Appendix A. All three geometric transformation modes were investigated and the results are shown in
Figure C.20.
Figure C.20: Buckling of cantilever beam subjected to axial loading
As expected, the linear geometric transformation mode reveals a linear load-deformation response as
no second order eﬀects are disregarded in this case. Te corotational transformation, on the other hand,
follows very well the analytical solution up to high forces and deformations. Terefore, non-linear geome-
tries are well modelled. Pdelta transformation gives results very similar of the ones of the corotational
transformation up to the magnitude of the critical load. Close to the critical load, the deformations start to
increase asymptotically and above the critical load, they switch into the opposite direction. Tis indicates
that the results given by Pdelta can be accepted for low and mid level loading, but inaccurate results might
be expected when loads approach the critical value.

C.3
Structural Fire Design Benchmark
Te structural ﬁre design validation is done by studying the behaviour of beam elements under tem-
perature loads and by comparing the results with analytical calculations.
Te main eﬀect of the ﬁre on the structure is that raising temperatures reduce the material properties
of steel structures. Both proportional limit, yield strength and modulus of elasticity are reduced during
rising temperature. Te response of a simple beam with diﬀerent boundary conditions was therefore in-
vestigated under rising temperatures to validate the material degradation models used by the sofware
OpenSees.
Temperature is applied in the FEM sofware to a structural element by deﬁning a temperature varia-
tion in function of the time. For this purpose the ﬁre curve given by ISO834 standard was used. In reality,
unprotected steel elements don’t necessarily reach the temperature of the ﬁre depending on the nature of
the ﬁre, or if they do so, there is a delay between the increase of the ﬁre and the steel temperature. Tis
phenomena is important in case of the main study, but during the benchmark no delays were considered.
Te benchmark was done in three steps. First, the ISO834 temperature curve was applied to a simply
supported beam, without any external loads and free movement of one of the supports. Te second step
was done using the same conﬁguration, but with uniformly applied vertical loads acting on the beam.
Te third exercise was a continuation of the previous one, in which second order p-delta eﬀects were also
considered in the analysis. Te same steps were done in parallel for a beam having similar geometry, but
with axially restrained supports. Table C.7 contains the main parameters of the investigated beam.
Table C.7: Fire benchmark properties
Proﬁle
Beam length [m]
Young’s Modulus [GPa]
Yield Strength [MPa]
IPE 300
5
210
275
Numerical results were computed using OpenSees for all three steps and for both boundary condi-
tions. Analytical methods were then used to validate the obtained results. Furthermore, the results were
compared with numerical results found by another FEM sofware, Abaqus in a previous study [34]. Te
chosen exercise was selected to be identical with the benchmark completed in this previous study, so a
second step of validation can be achieved.
C.3.1
Heated Beam
Te eﬀects of the temperature was ﬁrst studied on a simply supported and axially restrained beam
without any external loads, see Figure C.21. In such simple idealised conﬁguration, the temperature has
an eﬀect on the material properties only. Te material degrades and the element is free to expand linearly
due to the rolling support. As no other external forces are present, the expansion is fully supported by the
rolling supports. In theory the beam can extend without any limitations as there are no bending moments
to cause failure.

C.3.3
Heated Beam with Uniform Load and Geometric Non-linearity
Up to this point the equilibrium equations were writen for the model in the undeformed shape. To
generate a realistic response, the deformed shape has to be accounted for in the global stiﬀness matrix.
Figure C.25: Heated beam with uniform load and geometric non-linearity
Te response of the simply supported beam is similar to the case when no geometric non-linearity was
considered. Te only diﬀerence being just before failure when the horizontal displacements start to de-
crease due to the pulling-back eﬀect caused by the accelerated deﬂection of the beam. Te failure happens
due to reaching the bending capacity.
Te behaviour of the axially restrained beam is more complex in this case. Te bending moment slightly
increase at low temperatures due to the second order eﬀects. As the temperature increases so does the axial
force in the section, until a point where it starts to plasticize. Te compression force starts to decrease afer
this point and the bending moment starts to increase more rapidly. Te bending moment starts decreasing
when the eﬀect of the material degradation can be felt (at 400°C according to Eurocode).
Te vertical deﬂection increases continuously and at some point due to this, the compression forces
from the beam change to tension, pulling the two support nodes together. Tis phenomena is called the
catenary eﬀect. Te catenary eﬀect causes further decrease of the bending moments. Failure is no longer
caused by reaching the bending capacity but by deﬂection limitations. Terefore, it can be stated that non-
linear geometry has a favourable eﬀect in real life as although bending moments increase during some
range of the temperature history, but global failure occurs only at a later stage.

Fall Semester - 2019
Figure C.26: Structural response of heated beam with uniform load and geometric non-linearity

C.4.2.2
Elastic Time History Analysis
Te analytic solution of the time history analysis consists of solving the Duhamel integral as proposed
by Clough and Penzien [35].
Te same artiﬁcial accelerogram was used for the time history analysis of the MDOF as in case of the
SDOF system, presented in Figure C.27.
In addition to the previous, damping of the system was introduced in form of Rayleigh damping. Te
parameters of the damping a0 and a1, representing the participations of mass and stiﬀness proportional
damping were determined based on Section 2.2. Target damping was set for 2% and the obtained damping
deﬁnition is shown in Figure C.33.

Displacement response history at top of the frame was compared for the analytical and numerical
solution found by OpenSees in Figure C.34. It can be noticed that the solutions are very close to each other.
Tere are very minor diﬀerences between the two solution, which is due to the damping used. In case of the
analytical solution a ﬁx value of damping was used while in case of the numerical solution the Rayleigh
damping was used. Te Rayleigh damping consists of diﬀerent damping ratios for the diﬀerent modes
of vibration. Te Rayleigh damping was calculated to meet the target damping ratio used in analytical
solution for the fundamental frequency range of the structure, with minor variations in this area. Tis is
the reason for the minor diﬀerence between the two solutions.

==PAGE:148==
Appendix C - Benchmarks

Figure C.34: Damped elastic MDOF - Displacement response history
It can be observed, that the displacements start from 0 as the structure is in its initial state and ﬁnish
also at around 0. Te structure moves during the ground motion and enters in free oscillations when the
earthquake accelerations reduce. Tese oscillations are not ﬁnished at the end of the above graph, but it
can be seen that they have become stable and will be damped afer suﬃcient time.
As a conclusion, the time history analysis was conducted in the elastic region as no permanent deforma-
tions were recorded afer the earthquake. Tis is due to design of the structure which was suﬃciently stiﬀ
and robust to support all earthquake forces in the elastic region.
Another time history analysis was performed in order to investigate the inelastic behaviour of the
frame.
C.4.2.3
Inelastic Time History Analysis
Te inelastic time history analysis was performed in the same way as the elastic one, with the main
modiﬁcation of reducing the material’s strength. Te yield strength of the steel used was reduced by a
factor of 5 in order to see permanent deformations during the motion.
Furthermore, the Rayleigh damping was recalculated for a target damping of 0.5%, see Figure C.35.
Figure C.35: MDOF Rayleigh damping - inelastic

Figure C.36 shows the results of the inelastic time history analysis in comparison with the elastic one.

displacements of 25 mm were found at the end of the motions (afer oscillations ﬁnished).

C.5
Heat Transfer Benchmark
Te purpose of the heat transfer benchmark is to develop a heat transfer model for the main analysis
which is reliable representation of the reality. To achieve this a series of models from simple equivalent
section 2D to more complex 3D models were developed and will be presented in the following.
Te basis of the heat transfer benchmark was an IPE400 beam, similar to the ones from the ﬁrst level.
Such beam is exposed to ﬁre from all sides, but the top, where is it protected from the raising gas temper-
atures by the concrete slab, see Figure C.38.
Figure C.38: Beam exposed to ﬁre
Te section factor of the any element is calculated by the total area of exposed surfaces divided by its
total volume. If the exposure is constant, doesn’t change along the length of the element, then the section
factor is independent of the length of the element. Terefore, the section factor for the above mentioned
beam is Am
V
= 156.619.
Te temperature development of an unprotected and protected beam when exposed to ﬁre is based
on [7] and is shown in Figure 4.21. Te heat transfer benchmarks consists of building numerical models
which provide temperatures similar to the ones calculated analytically by the formulas indicated in [7],
Equations 4.2 and 4.3.
C.5.1
Main Parameters of the Heat Transfer Models
Te heat transfer models were created in the sofware Abaqus CAE 2018.
Afer creating the geometry of the model, the series of key parameters had to be deﬁned.
C.5.1.1
Materials
Two diﬀerent materials were used in the models: structural steel and ﬁre protection material. Each
of these two require three parameters, to be deﬁned: thermal conductivity, speciﬁc heat and density. Te
material properties for steel and insulation are described in Section 3.1.
Te materials are assigned to sections, which then are assigned to parts/elements. To be able to perform
the heat transfer analysis, solid homogeneous sections were selected for both 2D and 3D models.
145
==PAGE:151==
Appendix C - Benchmarks
Fall Semester - 2019
C.5.1.2
Element Type
Heat transfer DC2D4 - 4-node linear heat transfer quadrilateral elements were selected for 2D models
while, DC3D8 - 8-node linear heat transfer bricks were used for 3D models.
C.5.1.3
Boundary Conditions
Te beam is considered to be exposed from all sides, but the top, where the concrete slab prevents direct
convection or radiation from the surrounding ﬁre. Te ﬁre is applied to the element in terms of convection
and radiation from the surrounding gas. Terefore, it is not necessary to deﬁne any boundary conditions
on the surfaces where convection and radiation is applied. Abaqus considers adiabatic conditions when
no boundary conditions or no interactions are assigned to a speciﬁc surface. In order to simulate the slab
and create adiabatic surface at the top, no boundaries and neither any interactions were deﬁned for the
top surface of the beam.
Furthermore, in models were ﬁre protection was modelled, the contact between the structural steel
element and ﬁre insulation material was done in form of direct contact and inﬁnite conductivity between
meeting surfaces. Tis is possible to deﬁne in Abaqus by creating a Tie between the outer surface of the
steel element and the inner surface of the ﬁre protection material.
C.5.1.4
Analysis Steps
By default, an initial analysis step is created in Abaqus when opening the model. It is necessary to
apply the ambient temperature of 20°C in form of a predeﬁned ﬁeld at this step.
In addition, a heat transfer step needs to be added. It is necessary to deﬁne a transient analysis as we are
interested of the temperature development in the element as the ﬁre temperature increases. Te resulting
temperature development was used during the post-earthquake ﬁre analysis, therefore an increment of 1
second was deﬁned for the incremental steps.
In order to simulate the ﬁre, neither boundary condition nor thermal loads in terms of ﬂux is necessary.
Fire can be simulated by the deﬁnition of convective and radiative heat transfer mechanisms.
C.5.1.5
Convection
Convection can be applied as an interaction: surface ﬁlm condition. Figure C.39 shows the setings used
in Abaqus. Te ﬁlm coeﬃcient is the convective heat transfer coeﬃcient, for which a value of 25W/m2K
is recommended to be used by [7].
Te sink temperature is the ambient temperature, therefore the temperature of the gas (ﬁre) around the
element. Tis temperature can be deﬁned as a factor of 1 with an amplitude equal to the time dependent
temperature history of the ﬁre, given in Equation 4.1. In this case the ﬁre temperature development was
introduced as a XY data set.
146
==PAGE:152==
Appendix C - Benchmarks
Fall Semester - 2019
Figure C.39: Heat transfer - convection mechanism
C.5.1.6
Radiation
Similarly, radiation can be deﬁned as an interaction: surface radiation. Te setings are shown in Figure
C.40. [7] and [27] provide recommended values for the surface emissivity, ϵm = 0.7 and for the emissivity
of the ﬁre, ϵf = 1. Similarly, the ambient temperature has to be deﬁned, which in the current case is the
ﬁre gas temperature, given in Equation 4.1.
Figure C.40: Heat transfer - radiation mechanism
C.5.2
Equivalent Section
Te ﬁrst step of the heat transfer benchmark was to study the heat transfer mechanism on the simplest
model, a 2D rectangle having the same section factor as the studied beam. Te aim was to deﬁne convec-
tion, conduction and radiation heat transfer mechanisms in such way as to obtain the same temperatures
as from analytical calculation. Eurocode [7] formulas were used for the basis of the analytical calculations.
Te analytical calculation for some speciﬁc times studied are shown in Table C.18 for unprotected and
protected steel beam.

Appendix D
Appendix D - Sensitivity Analysis
Te accuracy of the results obtained by a ﬁnite element sofware is dependent on several modelling pa-
rameters. Assuming a correct model deﬁnition (including geometry, materials and loads) the results of the
FEM analysis can still experience small or larger diﬀerences from the exact solution depending on the set-
tings of parameters like element type, mesh size, element integration points and section integration points.
Sensitivity analysis was performed to investigate the inﬂuence of the above parameters on the ﬁnal
results and to reduce the level of uncertainty in the model.
D.1
Element Type
As presented in Appendix A, more than 100 element types exist in the library of OpenSees, each of
them having diﬀerent material and force-deformation assumptions. Te most applicable type of element
for beam-column type are the displacement and load based elements. Te main diﬀerence between them
is that the displacement based elements are based on approximate displacement ﬁelds and stiﬀness matrix
calculations, while the force based elements are derived from ﬂexibility matrix and exact force equilibrium.
For the present study the advantages of both types of elements were considered and displacement based
elements were chosen for the analysis as they can provide higher accuracy of the obtained displacement
results if suﬃciently high number of iterations are completed during analysis.
According to [36], force based elements are computationally more expensive. Force based elements
generally improve global and local response without mesh reﬁnement. In case of displacement based
elements, higher derivatives converge slower to the exact solution. Accurate local responses require ﬁner
ﬁnite element meshing than for accurate results of global responses.
D.2
Element Mesh
Meshing the elements means to divide each element of the original model into smaller ﬁnite size ele-
ments (generally of uniform size), leading to a much higher number of points and small elements. Element
mesh is necessary to obtain local FEM results close to the exact solution for any type of loading.
On one hand, no or coarse element meshing might fail to give accurate results. On the other hand as
meshing causes increase of the number of nodes and elements, it automatically causes higher computa-
tional cost as well. For example, meshing all elements in a general structure into two would approximately
double the computational cost. For ﬁner meshing the computational cost increases exponentially, there-
fore it is important to ﬁnd an adequate mesh size, for which the results are suﬃciently accurate and are
obtained from acceptable computational costs.
153
==PAGE:159==
Appendix D - Sensitivity Analysis
Fall Semester - 2019
Figure D.1 shows the sensitivity of the element meshing. Te beam with uniform load studied in the
plasticity benchmark was analysed with varying element mesh and the force-deformation graphs were
compared to the analytical solution. It can be seen that the element mesh size has a huge eﬀect on the
results.For displacement based elements it can be noticed that if less than 16 elements are used, the diﬀer-
ence between the computed and exact solution is signiﬁcant. Results for more than 16 elements provide
relatively good result and the exact number of elements can be chosen by further investigating the speed
of convergence to the exact solution.
Figure D.1: Sensitivity to mesh size
Figure D.2a and D.2b show the convergence to the analytically obtained value of force (at a reference
of 15 mm displacement) and the convergence to the displacement result (at a reference force of 253.8kN/m)
of the increasing number of elements used. It was decided that 20 elements provide suﬃciently accurate
results, with discrepancies less than 1% between the analytical and numerical solutions.
(a) Force convergence
(b) Displacement convergence
Figure D.2: Sensitivity to mesh size - Convergence
154
==PAGE:160==
Appendix D - Sensitivity Analysis
Fall Semester - 2019
Table D.1: Gauss-Lobato integration point locations
D.3
Element Integration Points
Another parameter investigated was the number of element integration points. Element integration
points are some points at speciﬁc locations along the element which represent the cross-sectional proper-
ties. Integrating results over these points gives the overall response of the element.
Each integration point has a location and weight, which is assigned depending on the integration type
used. OpenSees provides several numerical integration methods (for ex. Gauss-Lobato, Gauss-Legendre,
ﬁxed location, Newton-Cotes, etc.) to represent distributed plasticity. Te most widely used integration
type for beam-column elements is the Gauss-Lobato method, because it places two integration points at
each end of the element [37]. Integration points are relevant also because the sectional stresses and strains
can only be obtained in these points.
Between 3 and 10 integration points can be chosen for each element. A low number of integration
point might not give suﬃciently accurate stress distribution along the element, while a too high number
of integration points might produce some localisation, with overestimated values (due to the fact that
integration points are not exact solution but values which if interpolated along the length provide the
total element response). Table D.1 illustrates the locations and weights for 3-6 Gauss-Lobato integration
points.
Te exercise from the plasticity benchmark was repeated for diﬀerent number of integration points
and with ﬁx element size (20 elements). Te results are summarised in Figure D.3.
155
==PAGE:161==
Appendix D - Sensitivity Analysis
Fall Semester - 2019
Figure D.3: Sensitivity to number of element integration points
From the above and Figures D.4a and D.4b it can be seen that the results converge to the exact solution
very fast and 5 integration points are suﬃcient for the analysis. Tis is conﬁrmed by the study made
by [25], where a number of 5 integration points was proven with experimental tests to be suﬃcient for
displacement based beam-column elements.
(a) Force convergence
(b) Displacement convergence
Figure D.4: Sensitivity to number of element integration points - Convergence
156
==PAGE:162==
Appendix D - Sensitivity Analysis
Fall Semester - 2019
D.4
Fiber Integration Points
Te last investigated parameter was the number of ﬁber integration points along the height of the sec-
tion. Fiber sections are used to deﬁne any general geometry beam-column section when plastic material
is considered. Te ﬁbers are deﬁned by the user as a net covering the cross section or as horizontal strips
with given position and area. Te stress-strain results are calculated for each of the deﬁned ﬁbers.
Te number of ﬁbers along the height of the section has an eﬀect on the ﬁnal results. Along the
accuracy of the force-deformation relationships, ﬁber integration points eﬀect the stress-strain distribution
of the cross section. Figure D.5 shows a comparison between two cases when 5 and 11 sectional integration
points are considered. It is clear that for more integration points, a much beter distribution of the sectional
stresses can be seen. If less integration points are used, then the stresses and strains between the deﬁned
integration points are interpolated, therefore information in those regions are lost, see Figure D.5.
Figure D.5: Stress distribution with ﬁve vs. eleven ﬁber integration points
Figure D.6 contains the comparison of analysis done for diﬀerent number of ﬁber integration points,
having the same number of elements and section integration points.
157
==PAGE:163==
Appendix D - Sensitivity Analysis
Fall Semester - 2019
Figure D.6: Sensitivity to ﬁber integration points
Based on the results and the convergence Figures D.7a and D.7b a number of 11 integration points were
found to provide suﬃciently accurate results. Tis is conﬁrmed by the study [25], in which it is proven
with experimental tests that 6 ﬁber integration points are suﬃcient to obtain accurate global response and
higher number might be used to obtain a ﬁner distribution of responses along the section height.
(a) Force convergence
(b) Displacement convergence
(c) Sensitivity to ﬁber integration points - Convergence

[expr
$level*1000 + $bay*100 + $element]
381
set elemID [expr $level*1000 +$bay*100 + $element]
382
element dispBeamColumnThermal $elemID $nodeI $nodeJ $np
$BeamSecTag_IPE330 $IDBeamTransf -mass $QdlBeam_IPE330 -cMass;
383
} else {
384
set nodeI [expr
$level*1000 + $bay*100 + $element-1]
385
set nodeJ
[expr
$level*1000 + ($bay+1)*100 ]
386
set elemID [expr $level*1000 +$bay*100 + $element]
387
element dispBeamColumnThermal $elemID $nodeI $nodeJ $np
$BeamSecTag_IPE330 $IDBeamTransf -mass $QdlBeam_IPE330 -cMass;
388
}
389
}}}
